The Meaning of 'Taken with Negation'
Objection and answer defining 'taken with the condition of negation'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of 'abstract' in the context of 'taken with the condition of negation' and how they affect the possibility of existence.

Core Idea
The meaning of 'abstract' determines whether something can exist at all.

This passage addresses a critical objection about the phrase 'taken with the condition of negation'. The objector reduces it to mere mental abstraction. In response, Sanusi clarifies that there are two ways to understand 'abstract'—one that prevents existence, and one that allows it. This distinction is essential for understanding how logical concepts relate to reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and response
Read the passage and translation to understand the flow of argument. The objector challenges the definition, and the response provides a clarification.
Notice the back-and-forth structure.
2
Identify the two definitions of 'abstract'
The response distinguishes two meanings: (a) inherently free from all accidents, and (b) mentally abstracted from accidents. Find these in the text.
The first meaning leads to impossibility of existence; the second allows existence.
3
Understand the consequences of each definition
Consider how each definition affects the possibility of existence in the external world and in the mind. The second meaning permits existence in both realms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لا معنى للمأخوذ بشرط (لا) سوى ما يعتبره العقل كذلك . قلنا : فحينئذ لا يمتنع وجوده في الخارج بأن يكون مقروناً بالعوارض والمشخصات، ويعتبره العقل مجرداً عن ذلك، فصار الحاصل أنه إن أريد :بالمجرد ما لا يكون في نفسه مقروناً بشيء من العوارض مطلقاً، أو العوارض الخارجية امتنع وجوده في الخارج، وفي الذهن جميعاً، وإن أريد ما يعتبره العقل كذلك، جاز وجوده فيهما . فإن قيل : فكيف يصح على الأول الحكم بامتناع الوجود في الذهن؟ قلنا : شبهة المجهول المطلق وقد سبقت .
If it is said: There is no meaning for 'taken with the condition of negation' except what the intellect considers as such. We say: In that case, its existence in the external [world] is not impossible, in that it is combined with accidents and individuators, and the intellect considers it abstracted from that. So the result is that if what is meant by 'abstract' is that which is not in itself combined with any accidents absolutely, or external accidents, its existence is impossible both externally and in the mind. But if what is meant is what the intellect considers as such, its existence is possible in both. If it is said: How can the judgment of the impossibility of existence in the mind be valid according to the first [interpretation]? We say: [This is] the doubt of the absolute unknown, which has preceded.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning the definition of 'taken with the condition of negation' (al-ma'khudh bi-shart la). The objector claims that this phrase can only mean what the intellect considers as abstract. The response clarifies that even if the intellect considers something abstract, that does not prevent it from existing in the external world combined with accidents and individuators; it is merely mentally abstracted. The argument distinguishes two interpretations of 'abstract': first, something that is inherently free from any accidents, which cannot exist anywhere; second, something mentally abstracted from accidents, which can exist both in the mind and externally. When asked how the impossibility of mental existence is judged in the first case, the answer invokes a previous discussion about the 'absolute unknown' (shubhat al-majhul al-mutlaq), implying the issue has been resolved earlier.

Key Terms
المأخوذ بشرط لا
المجرد
العوارض
المشخصات
شبهة المجهول المطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'taken with negation' necessarily refers to something that cannot exist at all, but it can exist mentally or externally depending on interpretation.
Correction: The passage shows that 'abstract' can mean either inherently free from accidents (which cannot exist) or mentally abstracted (which can exist). The phrase 'taken with negation' is not self-contradictory; it can refer to things that actually exist but are conceptually abstracted.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens if 'abstract' is defined as that which is not combined with any accidents in itself?
A) It can exist in the mind only.
B) It can exist externally only.
C) Its existence is impossible both externally and in the mind.
D) It can exist both externally and in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The term 'abstract' in kalam has two meanings, and only one allows actual existence; the other is purely conceptual with no instantiation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new theological concept, do you ever mistake your mental abstraction of it for its full reality? How can this passage's distinction protect you from that error?
?
Have you ever assumed that something cannot be real because you cannot perfectly imagine it with all its details? Where else in your thinking about the unseen might this same logical slip occur?
?
The 'doubt of the absolute unknown' warns against declaring impossibility from mere ignorance. In your own life, where are you most tempted to say 'that cannot be true' simply because you haven't encountered it?

Refutation of Abstract Platonic Forms
Refutation of the claim that Platonic Forms exist as abstract entities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the doctrine of abstracted Platonic Forms is internally contradictory according to the kalam perspective.

Core Idea
An abstract entity cannot be receptive of opposites — that is the core of the refutation.

This passage engages with a famous philosophical claim attributed to Plato: that there exist abstract Forms (al-muthul) for every species. The author first clarifies what Plato really meant, then examines the apparent meaning and demonstrates its logical impossibility. You will follow how the concepts of 'quiddity without condition' and 'accidents' are used to expose the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author distinguishes between the apparent meaning and the true attribution to Plato.
Pay attention to the repeated phrase 'الماهية المجردة'.
2
Identify the two interpretations of Plato's Forms
The author first says the attributed view is about Forms in God's knowledge or as managing substances, not abstract entities. Then he considers the apparent meaning of abstract eternal individuals.
This sets up the refutation.
3
Understand the logical argument against abstractness
The key point: being 'receptive of opposites' requires having accidents, which contradicts 'abstractness'. Also, such an entity is a quiddity without condition, not one with condition of negation.
The author also notes that existence is an accident.
4
Grasp the conclusion: contradiction
The claim that abstract entities exist externally is self-contradictory because it combines incompatible attributes.
This is the core of the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما نقل عن أفلاطون يفيد ظاهره وجود الماهية المجردة عن اللواحق. قال: وما نسب إلى أفلاطون (۱) من المثل(۲)، ليس قولاً بوجود المجردة (۳)، بل بوجود الأنواع في علم الله تعالى (٤) أو بأن لكل نوع جوهراً(ه) مجرداً يدبر أمره بمنزلة النفس للبدن) . أنه . قد نقل عن أفلاطون ما يشعر بوجود الماهية المجردة عن اللواحق. وهو يوجد في الخارج لكل نوع فرد مجرد أزلي أبدي، قابل للمتقابلات . وأما التجرد وقبول المتقابلات فليصح كونه جزءاً من الأشخاص المتصفة بالأوصاف المتقابلة . وأما الأزلية والأبدية فلما سيأتي من أن كل مجرد أزلي، وكل أزلي أبدي . ولما كان هذا ظاهر البطلان. بناء على أن القابل للمتقابلات، والجزء من الأشخاص يتصف بالعوارض لا محالة وأنه هو الماهية لا بشرط شيء لا الماهية بشرط لا شيء، وأن الوجود من العوارض . فالقول بوجود المجردة تناقض .
What is narrated from Plato in its apparent meaning indicates the existence of the quiddity abstracted from attachments. He said: What is attributed to Plato of the Forms is not a statement about the existence of the abstract [entity], but about the existence of the species in the knowledge of God Most High, or that for every species there is an abstract substance that manages its affair in the manner of the soul for the body. Indeed. It has been narrated from Plato what suggests the existence of the quiddity abstracted from attachments, and it exists externally for every species as an individual abstract, eternal, everlasting, receptive of opposites. As for abstractness and receptivity to opposites, it would be valid for it to be a part of individuals that are described by opposite attributes. As for eternality and everlastingness, it is because of what will come: that every abstract is eternal, and every eternal is everlasting. And since this is apparently false, based on the fact that the receptacle of opposites and the part of individuals are inevitably characterized by accidents, and that it is the quiddity taken without condition (la bi-shart shay'), not the quiddity taken with the condition of negation, and that existence is among accidents, the statement about the existence of the abstract [entity] is contradictory.
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage refutes the claim that Platonic Forms are abstract entities existing externally. It begins by noting that certain reports from Plato suggest the existence of a quiddity (mahiyya) stripped of all attachments. However, the author immediately clarifies that what is truly attributed to Plato is not the existence of such abstract entities, but rather that the Forms exist in God's knowledge or are separate substances managing species. Despite this clarification, the passage then considers the apparent meaning of Plato's view as positing an eternal, abstract individual for each species that is receptive of opposites. The argument then dismantles this view: an entity that is both abstract and receptive of opposites cannot be logically consistent. If it is receptive of opposites, it must be subject to accidents, contradicting its abstractness. Furthermore, such an entity would be a quiddity without condition (la bi-shart shay'), not one taken with the condition of negation (bi-shart la), and existence itself is an accident. Therefore, the very claim of an abstract entity in the external world is self-contradictory.

Key Terms
الماهية المجردة
المثل
الجوهر المجرد
قابل المتقابلات
لا بشرط شيء
بشرط لا شيء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Platonic Forms exist as abstract individuals in the external world.
Correction: The author shows this is contradictory; rather, Forms are in God's knowledge or are managing substances.

Checkpoint
Why does the author consider the doctrine of abstract Platonic Forms contradictory?
A) Because abstract entities cannot be eternal.
B) Because an entity that is both abstract and receptive of opposites must have accidents, contradicting abstractness.
C) Because Plato himself denied their existence.
D) Because existence is not an accident.

Key Takeaway
The doctrine of abstracted Platonic Forms is contradictory because an entity cannot be both abstract and receptive of opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief about Allah or the unseen that was based purely on abstract reasoning, without grounding it in the Qur'an and Sunnah? How does this lesson's logical refutation of a widely accepted philosophical concept challenge you to re-examine your own assumptions? Commit to verifying one such belief this week with textual evidence.
?
The author uses precise terms to dismantle a false idea. When you hear a claim about Islamic theology, do you accept it at face value, or do you subject it to the criterion of revelation and sound reason? What is one concept you have accepted without scrutiny, and how will you test it starting today?
?
This lesson shows that even intellectually powerful ideas can be self-contradictory. In your own understanding of tawheed, is there any area where you feel tension or inconsistency? Write down one such area and decide today to seek clarity from a trusted scholar or reliable source.

Al-Farabi and Illuminationist Reinterpretations of Platonic Forms
Presentation of al-Farabi's and illuminationist reinterpretations of Platonic Forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Farabi and the illuminationists reinterpreted Plato's Forms to avoid positing eternal entities independent of God, and how they relate to divine knowledge and angelic lords of species.

Core Idea
Plato's Forms are reinterpreted either as God's knowledge or as angelic lords of species.

The critique of Platonic Forms led Muslim philosophers to offer alternative interpretations that avoid the problem of eternal co-existent entities. Al-Farabi identified the Forms with the unchanging ideas in God's knowledge. The illuminationists proposed that each species has a particular abstract substance—a 'lord of the species'—that governs it. This lesson presents these two reinterpretations and distinguishes them from the concept of suspended forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the two main interpretations and the conditional opening.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the discussion of suspended forms continues on the next page.
2
Identify the two interpretations
First, al-Farabi's view: Forms are eternal ideas in God's knowledge. Second, the illuminationist view: each species has a particular abstract substance called 'lord of the species'.
Both avoid Plato's problem of Forms co-existing with God.
3
Understand 'lord of the species'
The illuminationists claim each species has a governing abstract substance, e.g., the lord of fire preserves and illuminates it. They are called angels in religious language.
They are particular yet universal in the sense of equal effusion to all individuals.
4
Distinguish from suspended forms
The author distinguishes these lords from 'suspended forms' (al-muthul al-mu'allaqa), which are intermediate between sensible and intelligible and not specific to species.
This distinction is important for understanding the illuminationist ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللهم إلا أن تقيد العوارض بغير الوجود، أو يجعل الوجود نفس الماهية . قال الفارابي (۱) في كتاب الجمع بين رأي أفلاطون وأرسطو أنه إشارة إلى أن للموجودات صوراً في علم الله تعالى باقية لا تتبدل ولا تتغير. وقال صاحب الإشراق (۳) وغيره، أنه إشارة إلى ما عليه الحكماء المتألهون، عن أن لكل نوع من الأفلاك والكواكب والبسائط العنصرية (۳) ومركباتها جوهراً مجرداً من عالم العقول يدبر أمره (٤) ، حتى إن الذي لنوع النار هو الذي يحفظها وينورها، ويجذب الدهن والشمع إليها، ويسمونه رب النوع، ويعبر عنه في لسان الشرع بملك الجبال وملك البحار ونحو ذلك ومع الاعتراف بكونه جزئياً يقولون. إنه كلي ذلك النوع، بمعنى أن نسبة فيضه إلى جميع على السواء، لا بمعنى أنه مشترك بينها حتى يلزم أن تكون إنسانية مجردة، موجودة في الأعيان مشتركة بين جميع الأفراد محققة في المواد، فيكون هناك إنسان محسوس فاسد آخر معقول مجرد دائم لا يتغير أبداً. هذا غير. ثم لالمثل المعلقة التي يسمونها عالم الأشباح المجردة، فإنها لا تكون من الجواهر المجردة، بل كالواسطة بين المحسوس والمعقول، ولا تختص بأنواع
Unless accidents are restricted to other than existence, or existence is made identical to the quiddity. Al-Farabi said in the book 'The Harmony of the Opinions of Plato and Aristotle' that it is an indication that existents have forms in God's knowledge that remain, not changing or altering. The author of al-Ishraq and others said that it is an indication of what the theosophical philosophers hold: that for every species of the celestial spheres, planets, elemental simple substances, and their compounds, there is an abstract substance from the world of intellects that manages its affair, such that the one for the species of fire is what preserves it and illuminates it and attracts grease and wax to it. They call it the 'lord of the species,' and it is expressed in the language of divine law as 'the angel of mountains,' 'the angel of seas,' and the like. While acknowledging that it is particular, they say it is universal for that species, in the sense that the ratio of its effusion to all is equal, not in the sense that it is common among them such that there would have to be an abstract humanity existing in external individuals common to all individuals realized in matter, so there would be another sensible corrupt human and another intelligible abstract human everlasting, not changing at all. This is different. Then, as for the suspended forms that they call the world of abstract specters, they are not from the abstract substances, but rather like an intermediary between the sensible and the intelligible, and they are not specific to species.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage presents alternative interpretations of Plato's Forms that avoid the contradiction identified earlier. It begins with two possible exceptions: either accidents are restricted to exclude existence, or existence is considered identical to the quiddity. Then it reports al-Farabi's interpretation from his book 'Harmony of the Opinions of Plato and Aristotle': the Forms are actually the eternal, unchanging ideas of all beings in God's knowledge. The illuminationist philosophers (ishraqiyyun) offer a different interpretation: each species (of celestial bodies, elements, and compounds) has a particular abstract substance from the world of intellects that governs it – for example, the 'lord of the species' of fire preserves and illuminates it. These lords are referred to in religious language as angels of mountains, seas, etc. The illuminationists maintain that these lords are particular yet universal in the sense of equal effusion, not in the sense of a common universal Nature. The author then distinguishes this view from the doctrine of 'suspended forms' (al-muthul al-mu'allaqa), which are intermediate between sensible and intelligible and not specific to species. The passage treats these as distinct from the abstract substances.

Key Terms
المثل المعلقة
رب النوع
عالم الأشباح المجردة
صاحب الإشراق
الجمع بين رأي أفلاطون وأرسطو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might conflate the Platonic Forms with the 'suspended forms' or mistakenly think the illuminationist 'lords of species' are the same as universal abstract entities.
Correction: The illuminationists explicitly deny that the lord of the species is a common universal nature; it is particular in itself but universal in its equal effusion. Suspended forms are a different category, intermediate between sensible and intelligible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how do the illuminationists explain the universality of the 'lord of the species'?
A) It is a common nature inhering in all individuals.
B) Its effusion is equal to all individuals of that species.
C) It is a purely mental concept.
D) It is a sensible form.

Key Takeaway
Al-Farabi and the illuminationists reinterpreted Plato's Forms in ways that preserve Islamic theology: as God's knowledge or as angelic lords managing species.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you saw that al-Farabi localized the eternal Forms in God's knowledge. Have you ever truly internalized that every concept you study—justice, mercy, truth—exists perfectly and eternally in Allah’s knowledge? How does that awareness change the way you seek understanding in your daily life?
?
The illuminationists claim that every species, including fire, has a presiding 'lord'—an angel. Consider your own environment: the sun that gives light, the rain that falls, even the bacteria in your body. Do you truly believe each is commanded by a specific angelic being entrusted by Allah? If so, what responsibility does that place on you to honor those signs?
?
This passage distinguishes between a universal 'humanity' existing in matter and a separate abstract human—a view the author rejects. When you reflect on your own identity, do you sometimes abstract your 'self' from your actions, as if there is a pure essence untouched by your deeds? How might this lesson correct your view?

Objection and Answer: The Universal Natural's External Existence
Objection and answer on universal natural's external existence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the universal natural cannot exist externally due to particularization, and the response that distinguishes between the universal and its bearer.

Core Idea
The universal exists in the mind; its bearer exists externally.

A common philosophical puzzle: if the essence stripped of conditions is universal, how can it exist in the external world where everything is particular? The author addresses this by clarifying that what exists externally is not the universal as such, but the particular bearer of that universal nature. This distinction is crucial for understanding the relationship between mental concepts and extramental reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage carefully. The objector claims that the mahiyyah taken 'not-conditioned-by-anything' is a universal natural, which cannot exist externally because existence requires particularization, contradicting universality.
Note the key term 'kulli tabi'i' (universal natural).
2
Read the answer
Now read the second part. The author responds by stating that the universal natural is taken 'with the condition of being a subject for universality'. He then explains that its bearer (ma'rud) exists externally when particularized.
Pay attention to the phrase 'bishart kawnihi ma'rudan li-l-kulliyya'.
3
Understand the distinction
The key move is distinguishing between the universal natural itself (conceptual) and its bearer (the particular instance). The author concedes that universality as such does not exist externally, but the particular entity that the universal concept applies to does exist.
4
Summarize the conclusion
Form a clear takeaway: what exists externally is not the universal nature per se, but that of which the universal is predicated, namely the particularized individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء كلي طبيعي، فيمتنع وجوده العيني، ضرورة استلزامه التشخص المنافي للكلية. قلنا : لا ، بل الكلي الطبيعي هو المأخوذ ، بشرط كونه معروضاً للكلية، أنه موجود فمعناه أن معروضه الذي هو المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء موجود، وذلك عند عروض التشخص، وحاصلها أن (1) ما صدق هو عليه موجود).
For it is said: That which is taken not-conditioned-by-anything is a universal natural (kulli ṭabīʿī), so its external existence is impossible, necessarily entailing particularization which is incompatible with universality. We say: No, rather the universal natural is that which is taken with the condition of being a subject for universality, [but] it exists, so its meaning is that its bearer which is taken not-conditioned-by-anything exists, and that is when particularization occurs, and its summary is that that of which it is true exists.
Source pages p. 407

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the mahiyyah taken la bi-sharṭ shay' is a universal natural (kulli ṭabīʿī). As a universal, it cannot exist in the external world because external existence requires particularization, which contradicts universality. The author responds by distinguishing: the universal natural is indeed taken with the condition of being a subject for universality (sharṭ kawnihi maʿrūḍan li-l-kulliyya). However, its bearer (maʿrūḍuhu) — which is the mahiyyah taken la bi-sharṭ shay' — exists externally when it becomes particularized. Thus the summary is that the entity of which the universal is predicated exists, but the universal as such does not exist externally as a universal.

Key Terms
كلي طبيعي
تشخص
كلية
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the objector's argument stands and the universal natural cannot exist externally at all.
Correction: The author shows that while the universal as such does not exist externally, its bearer—the particular instance—does exist. Thus the universal natural is said to exist in a qualified sense.

Checkpoint
According to the author, the universal natural (kulli ṭabīʿī) is taken with what condition?
A) The condition of being not conditioned
B) The condition of being a subject for universality
C) The condition of existing externally
D) The condition of being particular

Key Takeaway
The universal natural does not exist externally as a universal; rather, its bearer—the particular individual—exists when it becomes particularized.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often think of ideals like justice or mercy as universal concepts. But when was the last time you took a specific action to make one of them a reality in your life? What particular step will you take today?
?
The lesson shows that a universal only exists through its particular bearer. In your own life, are you content with holding abstract beliefs without letting them take root in your actions? Confess one universal truth you claim to believe but have not made particular in your daily conduct.
?
The objector thought the universal could not exist externally. The author proved it could through particularization. Where have you been an 'objector' in your own heart—thinking a virtue is impossible to live out? What would it look like for you to be the 'bearer' of that virtue today?

Clarification: Absolute Essence is More General and Exists as the Concrete
Clarifies the relationship between absolute, mixed, and abstracted, and explains the existence of the absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the absolute essence (muṭlaq) is more general than both the mixed and abstracted, how it can coexist with its conditioned forms in extension despite conceptual distinction, and why it exists externally as the very concrete individual.

Core Idea
The absolute essence is not a separate part; it is the concrete individual itself.

The discussion of the absolute, mixed, and abstracted is central to understanding how essences exist. In this passage, the author clarifies that the absolute is more general, addresses a potential objection about incompatibility, and affirms that the absolute exists externally as the concrete individual, not as a separate part. Let's unpack this important clarification.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Note the three divisions and the objection-response structure.
The passage has a clear logical progression.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the definitions of mixed (makhluṭah), abstracted (mujarradah), and absolute (muṭlaqah). Notice that the absolute is said to be more general.
The absolute is taken 'la bi-sharṭ shay'.'
3
Understand the objection
The objection claims that conditioned and unconditioned are mutually exclusive. Identify why this objection arises and how the author responds.
The response distinguishes conceptual vs. extensional incompatibility.
4
Follow the resolution
The author explains that true extensional incompatibility is between being conditioned by something and being conditioned by its absence, not between conditioned and unconditioned.
Example: human conditioned by rationality vs. animal unconditioned by rationality.
5
Grasp the conclusion
The author rejects the view that the absolute exists as a part of the mixed. Instead, the absolute exists externally as the very restricted individual.
The absolute is predicated of the concrete individual.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لاخفاء في تباين المخلوطة (2) والمجردة، وأما المطلقة أعني المأخوذة لا بشرط شيء فأعم منهما لصدقه عليهما ضرورة صدق المطلق على المقيد . فإن قيل : المشروط بالشيء واللامشروط (3) به متنافيان، فكيف يتصادقان؟ . قلنا : التنافي إنما هو بحسب المفهوم، بمعنى أن هذا المفهوم لا يكون ذاك، وهو لا ينافي الاجتماع في الصدق كالإنسان المشروط بالنطق، والحيوان اللامشروط به وإنما التنافي في الصدق بين المشروط بالشيء، والمشروط بعدمه كالمخلوطة والمجردة، ثم لا نزاع في أن الماهية لا بشرط شيء موجودة في الخارج، إلا أن المشهور أن ذلك مبني على كونها جزءاً من المخلوطة الموجودة في الخارج، وليس بمستقيم لأن الموجود من الإنسان مثلا إنما هو زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من الأفراد وليس في الخارج إنسان مطلق، وآخر مركب منه ، ومن الخصوصية هو الشخص، وإلا لما صدق المطلق (4) عليه ضرورة امتناع صدق الجزء الخارجي المغاير، بحسب الوجود للكل، وإنما التغاير والتمايز بين المطلق والمقيد في الذهن دون الخارج . فلذا قلنا: إن (5) المطلق موجود في الخارج لكونه نفس المقيد ومحمـولاً عليه .
There is no concealment in the distinctness of the mixed (makhluṭah) and the abstracted (mujarradah). As for the absolute (muṭlaqah), i.e., that which is taken not-conditioned-by-anything, it is more general than both because it is true of both, necessarily the absolute being true of the restricted. For it is said: The conditioned by something and the unconditioned by it are mutually exclusive, so how can they be co-extensive? We say: The mutual exclusivity is only in terms of concept, meaning that this concept is not that, and that does not preclude coexistence in extension, like human conditioned by rationality, and animal unconditioned by it. Mutual exclusivity in extension is only between that which is conditioned by something and that which is conditioned by its absence, like the mixed and the abstracted. Then there is no dispute that the mahiyyah taken not-conditioned-by-anything exists in the external world, except that the well-known [view] is that this is based on its being a part of the mixed existing externally, but that is not sound, because what exists of, for example, human is only Zayd, ʿAmr, and other individuals, and there is not in the external world an absolute human and another [human] composed from it and particularity which is the person, for otherwise the absolute would not be true of it, necessarily impossible for the external part different in existence to be true of the whole. Rather the differentiation and distinction between the absolute and the restricted is in the mind, not in the external world. Therefore we said: The absolute exists in the external world because it is the very restricted and predicated upon it.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage clarifies the relationship between the three divisions and addresses a second objection. First, the author states that it is obvious that the mixed (makhluṭah) and the abstracted (mujarradah) are distinct from each other. The absolute (muṭlaqah), i.e., the mahiyyah taken la bi-sharṭ shay', is more general than both because it is predicated of both (since the absolute is true of the restricted). Then an objection is raised: the conditioned by something and the unconditioned by that same thing are mutually exclusive, so how can the absolute (unconditioned) and the mixed (conditioned by accidents) be co-extensive? The answer distinguishes between conceptual incompatibility and extensional compatibility. The conditioned and unconditioned are incompatible only conceptually (their meanings differ), but they can still apply to the same thing in extension, e.g., 'human' conditioned by rationality and 'animal' unconditioned by rationality both apply to humans. True extensional incompatibility exists only between that which is conditioned by something and that which is conditioned by its absence, such as the mixed (conditioned by accidents) and the abstracted (conditioned by the absence of accidents). After resolving this, the author affirms that there is no dispute that the mahiyyah la bi-sharṭ shay' exists externally. However, the common view—that it exists as a part of the mixed entity—is rejected. In external reality, we only find individuals like Zayd and ʿAmr; there is no separate absolute human plus a composite. If the absolute were a part, it could not be predicated of the whole (since a part is not true of the whole). Rather, the absolute and the restricted are distinguished only in the mind, not externally. Hence, the absolute exists externally as being the very restricted individual and predicated of it.

Key Terms
المخلوطة
المجردة
المطلقة
المشروط
اللامشروط
المقيد

Misconception: The absolute and the mixed are mutually exclusive in extension.
Correction: They are incompatible only conceptually; they can apply to the same thing in extension.

Checkpoint

B) It is incorrect because the absolute is not a part
C) It is uncertain
D) It applies only to abstracted essences

Key Takeaway
The absolute essence exists externally as the concrete individual, not as a separate part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your identity is not a separate essence plus accidental properties; you are the absolute manifested in your concrete, restricted existence. If every action and attribute defines the absolute you, then what does your daily life say about who you truly are? Will you change one habit today to align your actions with your deepest essence?
?
The lesson teaches that conceptual conflicts can coexist in reality. Think of a personal relationship where you see an irreconcilable difference. Is it truly an extensional conflict, or are you stuck in a conceptual opposition? How can you move from conceptual exclusivity to seeing the underlying unity?
?
If the absolute essence is exactly the concrete individual, then there is no room to say 'that's just my nature' to excuse a flaw—your nature is your choices. What specific flaw have you accepted as 'part of you' that you now realize you can change? Commit to one concrete step this week.

The Three Modes of Quiddity: Mixed, Abstract, and Absolute
The author defines the three modes of quiddity and resolves the contradiction between conditioned and unconditioned.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three modes of quiddity (mixed, abstract, absolute) and how the absolute can be true of both the mixed and abstract without contradiction.

Core Idea
The absolute quiddity is not contradictory with the conditioned; contradiction is only in the concept, not in existence.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid tackles a key logical distinction in kalam: the three ways a quiddity (essence) can be considered. The author explains how the 'absolute' or unconditioned quiddity can be more general than both the mixed and abstract, and resolves the apparent puzzle of how the conditioned and unconditioned can be simultaneously true. Let's break it down.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the concise style of the author; each sentence builds on the previous.
2
Identify the three modes
Find the terms makhluṭa (mixed), mujarrada (abstract), and muṭlaqa (absolute). Observe how the author defines the absolute as 'lā bi sharṭ shay' (without any condition) and states it is more general.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Read the objection about contradiction between conditioned and unconditioned. Note how the answer distinguishes contradiction in concept (mafhūm) from truth (ṣidq).
The example of 'human' (conditioned by speech) and 'animal' (unconditioned) helps clarify.
4
Grasp the distinction between concept and truth
The key is that two concepts can be different yet both true of the same thing. The real contradiction in truth is between opposites like mixed and abstract, not between absolute and conditioned.
5
See how the absolute exists externally
The author concludes that the absolute exists externally as the very same as the conditioned individuals (like Zayd and Amr), not as a separate part. The distinction is mental.
This resolves the apparent problem of how the absolute can be predicated of the conditioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا خفاء في تباين المخلوطة والمجردة، وأما المطلقة أعني المأخوذة لا بشرط شيء فأعم منهما لصدقه عليهما ضرورة صدق المطلق على المقيد . فإن قيل : المشروط بالشيء واللامشروط به متنافيان، فكيف يتصادقان؟ قلنا : التنافي إنما هو بحسب المفهوم، بمعنى أن هذا المفهوم لا يكون ذاك، وهو لا ينافي الاجتماع في الصدق كالإنسان المشروط بالنطق، والحيوان اللامشروط به وإنما التنافي في الصدق بين المشروط بالشيء، والمشروط بعدمه كالمخلوطة والمجردة، ثم لا نزاع في أن الماهية لا بشرط شيء موجودة في الخارج، إلا أن المشهور أن ذلك مبني على كونها جزءاً من المخلوطة الموجودة في الخارج، وليس بمستقيم لأن الموجود من الإنسان مثلا إنما هو زيد وعمرو وغيرهما من الأفراد وليس في الخارج إنسان مطلق، وآخر مركب منه ، ومن الخصوصية هو الشخص، وإلا لما صدق المطلق عليه ضرورة امتناع صدق الجزء الخارجي المغاير، بحسب الوجود للكل، وإنما التغاير والتمايز بين المطلق والمقيد في الذهن دون الخارج . فلذا قلنا: إن المطلق موجود في الخارج لكونه نفس المقيد ومحمولاً عليه .
There is no obscurity in the distinction between the mixed (makhluṭa) and the abstract (mujarrada). As for the absolute (muṭlaqa), meaning the one taken without any condition (lā bi sharṭ shay'), it is more general than both because it is true of both, necessarily by the truth of the absolute upon the conditioned. If it is said: The conditioned by something and the unconditioned by something are contradictory, so how can they be simultaneously true? We say: The contradiction is only according to the concept, meaning that this concept is not that concept, and this does not prevent their combination in truth, like 'human' which is conditioned by speech and 'animal' which is unconditioned by it. Rather, the contradiction in truth is between the conditioned by something and the conditioned by its absence, like the mixed and the abstract. Then there is no dispute that the quiddity (māhiyya) taken without condition exists in the external world, except that the well-known view is that this is based on its being part of the mixed which exists externally, and this is not sound because what exists of, for example, human, is only Zayd and Amr and other individuals, and there is not in the external world an absolute human and another composed of it and individuation which is the individual; otherwise, the absolute would not be true of it necessarily because it is impossible for an external part that is different according to existence to be true of the whole. Rather, the difference and distinction between the absolute and the conditioned is in the mind, not in the external world. Therefore we said that the absolute exists in the external world because it is the very same as the conditioned and predicated upon it.
Source pages p. 408

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the three modes of quiddity: mixed, abstract, and absolute. The absolute (unconditioned) is more general because it applies to both mixed and abstract. The author then addresses an objection: how can the conditioned and unconditioned be simultaneously true if they are contradictory? He clarifies that the contradiction is in the concept (mafhūm), not in external truth (ṣidq). For example, 'human' (conditioned by speech) and 'animal' (unconditioned by speech) can both be true of the same thing. The real contradiction in truth is between the conditioned by something and the conditioned by its absence (mixed vs. abstract). Then he affirms that the unconditioned quiddity exists externally, but not as a separate part. What exists are individuals (Zayd, Amr). The absolute quiddity is nothing but those individuals considered without added conditions. The distinction is mental, not external. Thus, the absolute exists externally as the very same as the conditioned and is predicated upon it.

Refuting the Claim: The Unconditioned as a Natural Universal
The author refutes the claim that the unconditioned cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the unconditioned (the taken without condition) is not automatically a natural universal, and how this refutes the objection that it cannot exist externally.

Core Idea
The unconditioned is not inherently a universal; it only becomes one with the accident of universality.

This lesson presents a key objection in the discussion of universals and external existence. The objector argues that the unconditioned (al-ma'khūdh lā bi-sharṭ shay') cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal. The author's refutation hinges on a precise distinction: the unconditioned by itself is more general than being a natural universal. Let us examine the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its reasoning
Read the objection carefully: the unconditioned is taken without condition, and the objector claims it is a natural universal, which cannot exist externally because existence entails individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) incompatible with universality.
Notice the logical form: universality and individuation are incompatible, so the unconditioned cannot exist externally.
2
Identify the author's key move
The author does not concede that the unconditioned is a natural universal. He distinguishes between the unconditioned taken merely as such, and the unconditioned considered as the subject (maʿrūḍ) of universality.
3
Understand the distinction
The unconditioned is more general than whether the accident of universality is considered or not. So it is not necessarily a universal; it only becomes a universal when the accident of universality is attached.
This is the critical point: the unconditioned can exist externally without that accident.
4
Reach the conclusion
Since the objection assumes the unconditioned is automatically a natural universal, the refutation shows that assumption is false. Therefore, the unconditioned's external existence is not impossible.
The incompatibility argument only applies if the unconditioned is a universal, but it is not necessarily so.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء يمنع أن يوجد في الخارج لأنه كلي طبيعي، ولا شيء من الكلي بموجود في الخارج، لأن الموجود في الخارج يستلزم التشخص المنافي للكلية، وتنافي اللوازم دليل على تنافي الملزومات . قلنا : لا : لا نسلم أن مجرد المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء كلي طبيعي، بل مع اعتبار كونه معروضاً للكلية، والمأخوذ لا بشرط شيء أعم من أن يعتبر هذا العارض أو لا يعتبر، فلا يمتنع وجوده.
If it is said: The one taken without any condition prevents its existence in the external world because it is a natural universal (kullī ṭabīʿī), and no universal exists in the external world, because what exists in the external world entails individuation (tashakhkhuṣ) which is incompatible with universality, and the incompatibility of necessary concomitants indicates the incompatibility of the things that entail them. We say: No: We do not concede that the mere taken without condition is a natural universal; rather, it is only with the consideration of its being the subject of universality (maʿrūḍ li'l-kulliyya). And the taken without condition is more general than whether this accident is considered or not, so its existence is not impossible.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector argues that the unconditioned (taken without condition) cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal (kullī ṭabīʿī), and no universal exists externally since external existence entails individuation (tashakhkhuṣ), which contradicts universality. The author replies that the unconditioned is not necessarily a natural universal. It is only when we consider it as the subject (maʿrūḍ) of universality that it becomes a universal. The unconditioned taken by itself is more general than whether the accident of universality is considered or not. Therefore, its external existence is not impossible.

تشخص
لا بشرط شيء
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The unconditioned is automatically a natural universal.
Correction: The unconditioned is only a natural universal when considered with the accident of universality; by itself, it is more general.

Checkpoint

B) He agrees it is a universal but says universals exist externally
C) He says the unconditioned is a universal only if considered with the accident of universality
D) He says the unconditioned does not exist externally

Key Takeaway
The unconditioned taken by itself is not a natural universal; it only becomes a universal when considered with the accident of universality, so its external existence is not impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector assumed the unconditioned is automatically a universal. Where in your life have you assumed a trait or situation is fixed when it might only be conditional? What would change if you distinguished between essence and accident in that area?
?
The author says 'we do not concede'—what is a belief you hold that you have never questioned because it seemed obvious? Are you willing to examine it with the same logical rigor as this passage?
?
The unconditioned's existence is not impossible. Think of a 'hopeless' situation you face. Are you adding an accidental condition (like 'this is impossible because...') that makes it seem impossible? How can the lesson's distinction free you to see possibilities you have overlooked?

The Existence of the Natural Universal's Subject
Author resolves an objection about the natural universal's external existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the subject of the natural universal and the accident of universality, and why the former exists externally while the latter does not.

Core Idea
The natural universal's subject exists externally, not the universal itself.

This passage addresses a common objection about the natural universal's external existence. The author clarifies a critical distinction between the subject of the natural universal and the accident of universality, resolving an apparent contradiction between logic and reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the passage slowly. First, identify the objector's argument: if universality is an accident incompatible with external existence, then the natural universal cannot exist externally, contradicting a well-known claim.
The objector is drawing a logical consequence from the previous discussion about the limitation of attributes.
2
Analyze the author's answer
Notice the author's key move: he distinguishes between the subject of the natural universal (taken without condition) and the accident of universality. The subject exists externally, but only when individuation occurs.
The Arabic terms are مهم: معروض (subject), عارض (accident), تشخص (individuation).
3
Understand the resolution
The final result: the 'mixed' quiddity (what the natural universal is true of) exists externally, but the composite of subject and universality (the intellectual universal) does not. This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : فينبغي أن لا يكون الكلي الطبيعي موجوداً في الخارج، لأن كلية العارضية تنافي الوجود الخارجي المستلزم للتشخص، وقد اشتهر فيما بينهم . أن الكلي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج. قلنا : معناه أن معروض الكلي الطبيعي وهو المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء موجود في الخارج، ووجوده الخارجي إنما يتحقق عند عروض التشخص، فيصير الحاصل أن ما صدق عليه الكلي الطبيعي، وهو المخلوط موجود في الخارج، وأما المأخوذ مع عارض الكلية، فلا يوجد في الخارج، كالمجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض والمسمى بالكلي العقلي.
If it is said: It would then be necessary that the natural universal does not exist in the external world, because the universality of the accident is incompatible with the external existence which entails individuation, and it is well-known among them that the natural universal exists in the external world. We say: Its meaning is that the subject of the natural universal, which is the one taken without any condition, exists in the external world, and its external existence only occurs upon the occurrence of individuation. So the result is that that to which the natural universal is true, which is the mixed, exists in the external world. As for the one taken with the accident of universality, it does not exist in the external world, like the composite consisting of the subject and the accident, which is called the intellectual universal (al-kullī al-ʿaqlī).
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage contains a further objection and answer. The objector says that based on the previous answer, the natural universal should not exist externally, but it is well-known that it does. The author answers by distinguishing between the subject (maʿrūḍ) of the natural universal and the accident of universality. The subject, which is the unconditioned quiddity, exists externally, but its existence is actualized only when individuation occurs. What is true of the natural universal (the mixed quiddity) exists externally. However, the quiddity taken together with the accident of universality (i.e., the intellectual universal) does not exist externally. This resolves the apparent conflict.

Key Terms

كلي عقلي
معروض
عارض
تشخص
مخلوط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The natural universal itself is a separate entity existing externally.
Correction: The natural universal as a universal does not exist externally; rather, its subject (the unconditioned quiddity) exists externally when individuated.

Checkpoint

A) The natural universal itself
B) The subject of the natural universal
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The subject of the natural universal exists externally when individuated, but the intellectual universal (universal as such) does not exist in the external world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that the natural universal's subject exists externally only when individuated. In your own life, do you mistake abstract concepts (like 'justice' or 'mercy') for concrete realities? How does this lesson challenge you to avoid conflating intellectual abstractions with real, individuated existences?
?
Consider a concept you deeply believe in—such as 'truth' or 'goodness'. Has your adherence to it become merely a mental universal, divorced from the messy, particular requirements of your daily actions? What would it mean for you to let that universal 'become individuated' in a specific situation this week?
?
The passage warns that treating the intellectual universal as externally existent is an error. Are there any 'intellectual universals' you hold onto that actually prevent you from engaging with the particular, real people and circumstances around you? Commit to identifying one such universal and intentionally violating its abstract rule in a concrete act of love or honesty.

Clarifying Ibn Sina's Condition of Nothing
Ibn Sina defines 'condition of nothing' mode of quiddity and clarifies a common misinterpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that Ibn Sina's phrase 'with the condition of nothing' for quiddity does not mean negating all accidental attributes, but rather taking quiddity without any further condition.

Core Idea
The 'condition of nothing' is not about stripping accidents; it's about not adding conditions.

Ibn Sina's classification of quiddity includes a mode called 'with the condition of nothing'. This might sound like quiddity stripped of all attributes, but the author quickly clarifies that this is not the intended meaning. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid a common misinterpretation that would render quiddity abstract and non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Ibn Sina is dividing quiddity with respect to conditionality.
The passage starts by stating the division and then defines one mode.
2
Identify the correct meaning
Find the phrase 'with the condition of nothing'. Understand that the author explains it does not mean conjoining no accident at all.
The condition is about not imposing an extra condition, not about negating all accidents.
3
Contrast with the wrong interpretation
Note the wrong interpretation: that all accidents are stripped. The author says this would make quiddity abstract (mujarrada) and non-existent.
This wrong interpretation is exactly what the passage guards against.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this distinction matters. It preserves the possibility of quiddity having accidents in reality while being taken without conditions in the mind.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الماهية باعتبار وجود الشرطية أو عدمها (قال: وذكر ابن سينا : أن الماهية قد تؤخذ بشرط لا شيء بمعنى أن ... وليس معنى شرط لا شيء اشتراط أن لا يضم إليها عارض أصلا فإن هذا المعنى يصيرها مجردة ولا وجود لها أصلا كما تقدم.
Division of quiddity with respect to the existence of conditionality or lack thereof. (He said: And Ibn Sina mentioned that quiddity may be taken with the condition of nothing (bi sharṭ lā shay'), meaning that... And the meaning of 'condition of nothing' is not the condition that no accident be conjoined to it at all, for this would make it abstract (mujarrada) and it would have no existence at all, as has preceded.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage introduces a classification by Ibn Sina. The author states that Ibn Sina divided quiddity (māhiyya) with respect to conditionality. One mode is taking quiddity 'with the condition of nothing' (bi sharṭ lā shay'). The author then explains that the meaning of this is not that all accidents are stripped, because that would result in an abstraction that has no existence. The condition 'with nothing' means taking quiddity without any additional condition, not that it is devoid of all attributes. This distinction is important for understanding the modes of existence of quiddity.

Key Terms
ماهية
بشرط لا شيء
مجردة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'with the condition of nothing' means quiddity is stripped of all accidental attributes (i.e., it is abstract and devoid of any accidents).
Correction: The author clarifies that if that were the meaning, quiddity would become abstract and would not exist at all. The actual meaning is that no additional condition (like universality) is stipulated, but accidents may still be present.

Checkpoint
What does Ibn Sina mean by taking quiddity 'with the condition of nothing'?
A) Stripping all accidents
B) Taking it without any additional condition but not necessarily stripping all attributes
C) Taking it as abstract
D) Taking it with all accidents

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's 'condition of nothing' means taking quiddity without any further condition, not stripping all accidental attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever reduced an important concept to a lifeless abstraction by stripping away all its attributes, thinking you were being precise? Ibn Sina warns that this leads to a non-existent reality. Commit to always consider the existence and concrete reality of what you study.
?
When you encounter a term like 'condition of nothing', do you assume it means something active and limiting? This lesson shows how a subtle misunderstanding can lead to a completely wrong conclusion. What other theological terms might you be misinterpreting in a similar way, and how can you guard against that?

Objection and Answer on the Natural Universal's External Existence
Objection and response about the existence of the natural universal in the external world.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection to the natural universal's external existence is answered by distinguishing between the substrate and the accident of universality.

Core Idea
The natural universal's existence is defended by separating its substrate from its accidental universality.

This passage presents a sophisticated dialectical exchange about why the 'natural universal' can exist in the external world despite its universality. The objection claims that external existence requires individuation, which contradicts universality. The author's response hinges on a distinction between the substrate (the 'thing taken without condition') and the accidental property of universality. Let's follow the argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Begin by reading the objection presented: the thing taken without condition cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal, and no universal exists externally due to the requirement of individuation.
Notice the logical structure: contradiction of concomitants indicates contradiction of subjects.
2
Identify the author's first response
The author denies that the mere thing taken without condition is a natural universal; it is a substrate that may or may not have the accident of universality. This broadens the concept.
The 'taken without condition' is broader than the natural universal.
3
Understand the second response
The author clarifies that when the natural universal is said to exist externally, it means its substrate (the mixed reality) exists with individuation, not the substrate with the accident of universality (the intellectual universal).
This resolves the apparent contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء يمنع أن يوجد في الخارج لأنه كلي طبيعي، ولا شيء من الكلي بموجود في الخارج، لأن الموجود في الخارج يستلزم التشخص المنافي للكلية، وتنافي اللوازم دليل على تنافي الملزومات . قلنا : لا نسلم أن مجرد المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء كلي طبيعي، بل مع اعتبار كونه معروضاً للكلية، والمأخوذ لا بشرط شيء أعم من أن يعتبر هذا العارض أو لا يعتبر، فلا يمتنع وجوده. مع أن الكلي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج، لأن كلية العارضية تنافي الوجود الخارجي المستلزم للتشخص، وقد اشتهر فيما بينهم أن الكلي الطبيعي موجود في الخارج. قلنا : معناه أن معروض الكلي الطبيعي وهو المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء موجود في الخارج، ووجوده الخارجي إنما يتحقق عند عروض التشخص، فيصير الحاصل أن ما صدق عليه الكلي الطبيعي، وهو المخلوط موجود في الخارج، وأما المأخوذ مع عارض الكلية، فلا يوجد في الخارج، كالمجموع المركب من المعروض والعارض والمسمى بالكلي العقلي.
If it is said: The thing taken without condition cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal, and no universal exists externally, because what exists externally necessarily entails individuation which contradicts universality, and the contradiction of necessary concomitants indicates the contradiction of the things they accompany. We say: No, we do not grant that the mere thing taken without condition is a natural universal; rather, it is considered as a substrate for universality. The thing taken without condition is broader than whether this accident is considered or not, so its existence is not impossible. Moreover, the natural universal exists externally, because the universality of the accident contradicts external existence which entails individuation, yet it is well-known among them that the natural universal exists externally. We say: Its meaning is that the substrate of the natural universal, which is the thing taken without condition, exists externally, and its external existence only occurs when individuation accrues. So the result is that what the natural universal is predicated upon – namely the mixed – exists externally. As for that which is taken with the accident of universality, it does not exist externally, like the composite whole of substrate and accident, called the intellectual universal.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange (objection and answer) between an opponent and the author regarding the existence of the 'natural universal' (al-kullī al-ṭabīʿī) in the external world. The objection states that the 'thing taken without condition' (al-maʾkhūdh lā bi-sharṭi shayʾ) cannot exist externally because it is a natural universal, and no universal exists externally—external existence requires individuation (al-tashakhkhuṣ) which contradicts universality. The contradiction of concomitant properties proves the contradiction of the subjects themselves. The author responds by first denying that the mere 'taken without condition' is a natural universal; rather, it is considered as a substrate (maʿrūḍ) for universality. The 'taken without condition' is broader—it may or may not have the accident of universality, so its existence is not impossible. The opponent then raises a second objection: if the natural universal exists externally, then its universality (which is accidental) would contradict external existence requiring individuation. The author answers that what is meant by the natural universal's external existence is that its substrate—the 'taken without condition'—exists externally, but its external existence occurs only when individuation attaches. Thus, what the natural universal is predicated upon (the 'mixed' reality) exists externally, whereas the substrate considered with the accident of universality (the 'intellectual universal') does not exist externally, just as a composite of substrate and accident does not exist as a composite in external reality.

Key Terms
المأخوذ لا بشرط شيء
كلي طبيعي
تشخص
كلي عقلي
معروض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The thing taken without condition (al-maʾkhūdh lā bi-sharṭi shayʾ) is itself a natural universal.
Correction: The author clarifies that it is merely a substrate for universality, which can be considered with or without the accident of universality, so it is not necessarily a universal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the 'thing taken without condition' (al-maʾkhūdh lā bi-sharṭi shayʾ)?
A) A natural universal that cannot exist externally.
B) A substrate that can be considered with or without the accident of universality.
C) An intellectual universal that exists only in the mind.
D) A particular existing individual.

Key Takeaway
The natural universal exists externally only through its substrate when individuation occurs, not as a universal, resolving the apparent contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that an abstract concept like 'justice' or 'truth' is actually a real thing out there, and felt confused when reality didn't match your ideal? How does the author's distinction between substrate and accident change the way you think about such concepts? What is one abstract idea you need to stop treating as an external reality starting today?
?
The opponent in this lesson saw a contradiction between universality and external existence. Are there contradictions in your own life between what you believe and what you experience? How can the method of separating the substrate from the accident help you find a resolution? Identify one specific contradiction you face, and commit to resolving it this week by applying this analytical approach.

Ibn Sina's Threefold Division of Essence
Ibn Sina's threefold division of essence: with condition, without condition, and absolute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three ways essence can be considered according to conditionality as formulated by Ibn Sina.

Core Idea
Essence is defined by what it includes or excludes.

Ibn Sina's division of essence by conditionality is a foundational logical tool. It distinguishes how an essence relates to its concomitants, whether they are excluded, allowed, or neither. This lesson presents the three categories: 'with the condition of nothing,' 'not with this condition,' and 'without condition,' and explains their logical roles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the first part where the threefold division is defined.
Notice the technical terms: بشرط لا شيء, لا بهذا الشرط, لا بشرط شيء.
2
Identify the three conditions
Find the phrases for each condition and note how Ibn Sina describes the logical status of essence under each.
Important: 'without condition' (لا بشرط) splits into two subcases: genus and species.
3
Understand the example of animal and man
The passage uses 'animal' as an example. See how 'animal' is a material part of 'man' when conditionally excluding 'rational', and a genus when absolute.
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to test your grasp of the definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم الماهية باعتبار وجود الشرطية أو عدمها (قال: وذكر ابن سينا: أن الماهية قد تؤخذ بشرط لا شيء بمعنى أن يزيد عليها كل ما يقارنها فتكون مادة للمجموع متقدمة عليه في الوجودين، ممتنعة الحمل عليه ضرورة لزوم اتحاد الموضوع والمحمول في الوجود، وقد تؤخذ لا بهذا الشرط بل مع تجويز أن يقارنها غيرها، وأن لا يقارنها وحينئذ إن كانت مبهمة محتملة للقولية على مختلفات الحقائق غير متحصلة بنفسها، بل بما ينضاف إليها، فتجعلها أحد تلك المختلفات فجنس، والمنضاف فصل، وإن كانت متحصلة بنفسها أو بما انضاف إليها فنوع فالحيوان بشرط أن لا يدخل فيه الناطق، مادة للإنسان جزء له غير محمول عليه، وبشرط أن يدخل نوع وهو الإنسان نفسه، ولا بشرط أحدهما جنس له محمول عليه - فلا يكون جزءاً له، وإنما يقال له الجزء لما يقع جزءاً من حده، ضرورة أنه لا بد للعقل من ملاحظته في تحصيل صورة الإنسان، وأما في الخارج فمتأخر، ضرورة أنه ما لم يوجد الإنسان لم يعقل له شيء يعمه وغيره). ما ذكرنا من معنى الماهية بشرط شيء وبشرط لا شيء، ولا بالشرط هو المشهور فيما بين المتأخرين وذكر ابن سينا أن الماهية قد تؤخذ لا شيء بأن يتصور معناه بشرط أن يكون ذلك المعنى وحده، ويكون كل بشرط ما يقارنه زائداً عليه، ولا يكون المعنى الأول مقولاً على ذلك المجموع حال المقارنة جزءاً منه مادة له، متقدماً عليه في الوجود الذهني والخارجي ضرورة امتناع تحقق الكل بدون الجزء ويمتنع حمله على المحمول لانتفاء شرط
Division of essence with respect to the existence of conditionality or its absence. (He said: Ibn Sina mentioned that essence may be taken with the condition of nothing, meaning that everything that accompanies it is added to it, so it becomes a matter for the composite, prior to it in both existences, impossible to predicate of it necessarily due to the necessity of the unity of subject and predicate in existence. And it may be taken not with this condition, but with the permissibility of something else accompanying it or not, and then if it is ambiguous, capable of being said of different realities, not determined by itself but by what is added to it, then it makes it one of those different things, so it is a genus, and what is added is a differentia. And if it is determined by itself or by what is added to it, then it is a species. So animal with the condition that rational not enter into it is a matter for man, a part of it not predicated of it; and with the condition of entering, it is a species, which is man himself; and without either condition, it is a genus of it, predicated of it, so it is not a part of it. It is only called 'part' because it falls as a part of its definition, necessarily, for the intellect must observe it in obtaining the form of man, but externally it is posterior, necessarily, for as long as man does not exist, nothing that encompasses him and others is conceived for him.) What we mentioned of the meaning of essence with a condition, with the condition of nothing, and without condition is well-known among the later scholars. Ibn Sina mentioned that essence may be taken as 'not a thing' by conceiving its meaning with the condition that that meaning alone, and everything with the condition of what accompanies it is additional to it; and the first meaning is not predicated of that composite at the time of accompaniment as a part of it, a matter for it, prior to it in mental and external existence necessarily due to the impossibility of the whole achieving without the part, and its predication on the predicate is impossible due to the absence of the condition.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's division of essence (al-māhiyya) according to conditionality. It describes three ways essence is considered: (1) 'with the condition of nothing' (bi-sharṭi lā shayʾ): here, all extra concomitants are excluded; the essence becomes a matter (mādda) for the composite, prior to it in both mental and external existence, and cannot be predicated of the composite without contradiction. (2) 'Not with this condition' (lā bi-hādhā al-sharṭ), i.e., absolute: it allows for accompaniment or lack thereof. In this case, if the essence is ambiguous and not determined by itself, it functions as a genus (jins) for different species, with the added determination being a differentia (faṣl). If it is already determined (by itself or by addition), it is a species (nawʿ). Examples: 'animal' with the condition of 'non-rational' is a material part of 'man' not predicated of it; with the condition of 'rational' (i.e., entering as species) it is man himself; without either condition (absolute), it is a genus predicated of man, not a part. The passage then notes that this threefold division (with condition, with condition of nothing, and without condition) is well-known. It adds that Ibn Sina also mentions taking essence as 'not a thing' (lā shayʾ), meaning the essence is considered alone without any condition, so whatever accompanies it is additional, and the first meaning is not predicated of the composite as a part or matter, but is prior to it; predication is impossible due to the lack of the condition. The text is cut off mid-sentence.

Key Terms
الماهية
بشرط لا شيء
لا بشرط شيء
جنس
نوع
فصل
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'with the condition of nothing' means the essence is completely bare and has no relation to anything.
Correction: It means the essence is taken alone, but it still serves as a material part for the composite; it is prior and not predicated.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina's division, when essence is taken 'with the condition of nothing', what is its relation to the composite?
A) It is predicated of the composite as a genus.
B) It is a material part of the composite, prior to it and not predicated.
C) It is the composite itself as a species.
D) It is an accident added to the composite.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina divides essence into three conditional categories, with 'with the condition of nothing' being a material part, and 'not with this condition' allowing for genus or species depending on determination.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider how you define yourself: Do you think of your essence as something pure and stripped of all roles and attributes ('with condition of nothing'), or as something that includes your actions and beliefs? How does this affect your view of your own identity and accountability?
?
The passage distinguishes between essence as a genus (universal and ambiguous) and as a species (determined). When you describe yourself to others, do you tend to use generic labels or specific ones? Which is more truthful to your reality, and which do you rely on for self-worth?
?
If your essence is taken 'without condition' — not excluding or including anything — then everything extra is accidental. What are the 'accidents' in your life that you treat as if they were essential? What would you lose if you stripped them away?

The Well-Known Division and the 'Not a Thing' Interpretation
Further elaboration on the division and the 'not a thing' interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the additional nuance of 'not a thing' and how it relates to the well-known threefold division.

Core Idea
The 'not a thing' interpretation reinforces the priority of essence as a part.

After presenting the threefold division, Ibn Sina adds a nuance: the essence can be taken as 'not a thing' (lā shay') meaning it is considered alone without any condition. This further clarifies how the essence functions as a prior part in a composite and cannot be predicated. The passage also notes that this threefold division became standard among later scholars.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for this part. Note the connecting phrase 'ما ذكرنا' which refers back to the previous division.
2
Understand the 'not a thing' interpretation
This is a restatement emphasizing that the essence alone is the first meaning, and everything added is additional.
It aligns with 'with the condition of nothing' but highlights the priority.
3
Notice the predication issue
The passage explains why this first meaning cannot be predicated of the composite when accompaniments are added.
4
Historical note
The division is said to be well-known among later scholars, showing its influence.

The Three Modes of Quiddity: Bi-shart, Bi-shart la, and La bi-shart
Defines the three modes of quiddity and explains their logical roles

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three modes of quiddity (mahiyya) and how they determine whether quiddity functions as matter, species, or genus in logical composition.

Core Idea
The three modes of quiddity are the key to understanding how genus, species, and differentia relate in logical definition.

This passage from Sanusi establishes a foundational logical concept: that a quiddity (essence) can be considered in three ways depending on whether we include or exclude accompaniments. These modes determine whether the quiddity serves as matter (part), species (whole), or genus (predicated) in the definition of a thing. Understanding this distinction is essential for grasping how definitions work in Aristotelian logic.

Guided Path
1
Understand the three conditions
Identify the three modes: 'with condition of something', 'with condition of nothing', and 'without condition'. Note how each condition affects the quiddity's relationship to accompaniments.
The Arabic terms are: بشرط شيء, بشرط لا شيء, لا بشرط.
2
See the logical roles
For each mode, observe whether the quiddity becomes matter (part), species (whole), or genus (predicated). The passage explains these roles using the example of animal and man.
The roles are based on whether the quiddity is predicated of the composite or not.
3
Apply the example
Work through the three ways animal is taken: as matter for man (bi-shart la), as man himself (bi-shart shay'), and as genus (la bi-shart). See how each corresponds to a different logical function.
This concretizes the abstract definitions.
4
Grasp the part vs. predication distinction
Understand that genus and differentia are called 'parts' only in the definition, not in reality. The mind requires them to conceive the species, but they are not ontological components.
This addresses a common misunderstanding.
