(۲) بمعنى أن الماهية يفتقر وجودها الذهني والخارجي إلى وجود كل جزء من أجزائها حتى لو انتفى واحد من أجزائها انتفت إذ لا وجود لكل بدون الجزء فعدمها يكفي فيه عدم واحد من أجزائها ووجودها لا بد له من كل جزء. (۳) أي لا يحتاج إلى وسط وبرهان يتوصل به إلى التصديق بثبوت ذلك الجزء للماهية في الذهن. (٤) أي إنما ثبتنا للجزء بالإضافة إلى بعض ما هو خارج عن الماهية كطول بناء ؛ أي طويلاً يلاقي نفسه في السماء مقدار ألف ذراع مثلاً . فإن إدراك كون طوله هذا القدر يفتقر إلى وسط هندسي ومساحي يدرك به كقدره كبياض الجسم في الجملة فقد يفتقر إلى وساطة هو للقصار مثلا على ما تقدم وإلا فبعض ما هو خارج عن الماهية ثبتت له هاتان الخاصيتان .
It is required for the composite that its parts precede mentally and externally. (He said: And there is no escape from the part preceding mentally and externally (2), so it entails independence from the middle term in assent (3), and from the intermediate [cause] in existence, except that the first property is real, and the other two are relative. Meaning: the part of a thing precedes it in existence and non-existence in the mind and externally. As for existence, it is relative to each part; as for non-existence, it is relative to some one of the parts. That is, the existence of man, for example, in the mind depends on the existence of animal and rational, and its non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them. And the existence of a house externally depends on the existence of wall and roof, and its non-existence depends on the non-existence of one of them. And from the first branches the independence from the middle term in assent, meaning that the mind's judgment about the belonging of the essential to the quiddity does not depend on observing a middle term and acquisition through demonstration, but rather it must be affirmed for it, and it is impossible to deny it from it merely by conceiving it. And from the second [branches] the independence from the middle [cause] in existence, meaning that the attainment of the part for the composite, like the wall for the house and the color for blackness, does not depend on a new cause; for the maker of the wall is the maker of the house, and the maker of the color is the maker of the blackness. (1) The word 'originally' is omitted from manuscript (أ). (2) Meaning that the quiddity's mental and external existence depends on the existence of each of its parts, so if one of its parts ceases, it ceases; for there is no existence of the whole without the part, so its non-existence suffices by the non-existence of one of its parts, and its existence requires every part. (3) Meaning it does not need a middle term and demonstration by which to reach assent to the belonging of that part to the quiddity in the mind. (4) Meaning we only affirm for the part in relation to something external to the quiddity, like the length of a building, i.e., a tallness reaching the sky of a thousand cubits, for example. For perceiving that this length is of this amount depends on a geometrical and mensural middle term by which to perceive its magnitude, like the whiteness of a body in general; it may depend on the mediation of something, like the short for example, as previously mentioned; otherwise, some of what is external to the quiddity has these two properties affirmed for it.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage presents a rule and its derived consequences. It begins by stating the condition for any composite (مُرَكَّبَة): its parts must be prior to it both mentally (ذهناً) and externally (خارجاً). The author then quotes the original text (قَالَ) which asserts that the part must precede in both mind and external existence, and that this priority entails two kinds of independence: independence from a middle term in assent (الاستغناء عن الوسط في التصديق) and independence from an intermediate cause in existence (الواسطة في الثبوت). However, the first property (independence in assent) is real (حقيقية), while the other two (the priority itself and independence in existence) are relative (إضافيتان).

Then a detailed explanation follows: The part precedes the whole in both existence and non-existence, in mind and externally. 'Existence' means that the whole's existence depends on the existence of each part; 'non-existence' means that the non-existence of any one part suffices for the non-existence of the whole. Examples: the existence of man in the mind requires both animal and rational; his non-existence follows from the absence of either. Similarly, a house requires both wall and roof; if either is absent, the house does not exist.

From the mental priority (the first property), we derive independence from a middle term in assent: the mind's judgment that an essential (ذاتي) belongs to a quiddity (ماهية) does not require a middle term or demonstration. It is known directly upon conceiving the quiddity. For example, 'animal' is essential to 'man'; we know immediately that man is animal without proof. Denying it is impossible once the concepts are understood.

From the external priority (the second property), we derive independence from an intermediate cause in existence: the part's existence for the composite does not require a separate cause. The maker of the part is the maker of the whole. For example, the same maker who makes the wall makes the house; the same agent who produces the color produces the blackness.

The passage concludes with textual notes and explanatory footnotes: one manuscript omits 'originally' (أصلاً) in a related context; the meaning of priority is clarified as the dependency of the quiddity's existence on each part; and a note about affirming additional properties (like length) that may require a middle term, which is outside the scope of this essential relation.

Key Terms
الواسطة في الثبوت (al-waasita fi al-thuboot)
خاصة حقيقية (khaassa haqiiqiyya)
خاصتان إضافيتان (khaassataan idaafiyyataan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the part and the whole require separate causes, e.g., the wall is made by a different agent than the house.
Correction: The passage asserts that the same cause suffices for both part and whole; no new cause is needed for the part to exist in the composite.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the wall not require a separate cause from the house?
A) Because the wall is not a true part of the house.
B) Because the maker of the wall is the maker of the house.
C) Because the wall exists independently of the house.
D) Because the wall is an accidental property.

Key Takeaway
The part's existence within the composite does not require a separate cause; the cause of the whole is also the cause of the part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the cause of the whole is also the cause of every part, why do you often attribute your successes in small matters to your own effort while acknowledging God only in the big picture? How can you change that?
?
Think of a relationship or project that feels like it is made of many separate parts. Does this lesson challenge you to see God's sole causality in each detail? What would it mean to trust Him for the tiny intermediate steps?
?
When you pray for guidance (the whole), do you also trust God for the specific moments (the parts) that lead to that guidance, or do you rely on your own planning? How can you actively surrender both to Him?

The Three Properties of a Part
Al-Taftazani enumerates and explains the three special properties of a part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three special properties of a part (juz') and the distinction between real and relational properties in kalam ontology.

Core Idea
The part has three properties: priority, independence in assent, and independence in existence.

In classical Islamic logic and ontology, the concept of a 'part' (juz') is fundamental. Al-Taftazani here enumerates three properties that are unique to parts, distinguishing them from accidents and other entities. Understanding these properties is crucial for grasping how predicates attach to subjects in necessary propositions and primary assertions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Taftazani uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three properties
The passage presents three properties of a part. Find each one and notice how Al-Taftazani defines it and whether it is real or relational.
The first property is priority; the second is independence from an intermediary in assent; the third is independence from a middle in existence.
3
Distinguish real vs. relational
Al-Taftazani explicitly marks the first property as real (ḥaqīqiyya) and the other two as relational (iḍāfiyya). Ask yourself why the first is real and the others not.
Real properties are intrinsic to the part; relational depend on a relation to something else.
4
Analyze the relation between the second and third
The author explains the difference between independence from an intermediary in assent (second) and independence from a middle in existence (third). He also describes a general-special relation (umūm min wajh) between them.
Try to think of examples: the equality of triangle angles to two right angles requires a middle in proof but is still necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فظهر أن للجزء خواصاً ثلاثاً : الأولى : التقدم في الذهن والخارج وهي خاصة حقيقية لا تصدق على شيء من العوارض . الثانية : الاستغناء عن الواسطة في التصديق، بمعنى وجوب الثبوت، وامتناع السلب بمجرد إخطار الجزء والماهية بالبال بل بمجرد تصور الماهية وهذه خاصة إضافية لا حقيقية، لصدقها على اللوازم البيئة بالمعنى الأعم، إن اشترط اخطارهما، والأخص إن اكتفى بتصور الماهية. الثالثة : الاستغناء عن الوسط في الثبوت، وهي أيضاً إضافية، لصدقها على الأعراض الأولوية، أعني اللاحقة للشيء لذاته من غير واسطة، سواء كان الجزم بثبوتها للموضوع، محتاجاً إلى وسط كتساوي الزوايا الثلاث للقائمتين بالنسبة إلى المثلث فإنه لازم له لذاته، ويفتقر بيانه إلى وسائط أو غير محتاج كالانقسام بالمتساويين للأربعة والبيض بسطح الجسم الأبيض فالاستغناء عن الوسط يجعل القضية أولية، والاستغناء عن الواسطة يجعل محمولها أولياً، وبينهما عموم من وجه لتصادقهما في انقسام الأربعة، وبياض السطح، وصدق الأولى بدون الثانية في بياض الجسم، وبالعكس في تساوي زوايا المثلث للقائمتين
Thus it appears that the part (juz') has three properties: First: priority in the mind and external existence, and it is a real (ḥaqīqiyya) property, not applying to any of the accidents. Second: independence from the intermediary (wāsiṭa) in assent, meaning necessity of affirmation and impossibility of negation merely by bringing the part and the quiddity (māhiyya) to mind, rather merely by conceiving the quiddity. This is a relational (iḍāfiyya) property, not real, because it applies to the evident concomitants (lawāzim bayyina) in the general sense if their bringing to mind is stipulated, and in the specific sense if mere conception of the quiddity suffices. Third: independence from the middle (wasṭ) in existence, and it is also relational, because it applies to primary accidents (a'rāḍ awwaliyya), meaning those that follow the thing by itself without intermediary, whether certainty of their attribution to the subject needs a middle, like the equality of the three angles of a triangle to two right angles, for it is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the triangle by itself but its demonstration needs intermediaries, or does not need, like the divisibility into equal halves of four, and the whiteness of the surface of a white body. Thus, independence from the middle makes the proposition primary (awwaliyya), and independence from the intermediary makes its predicate primary, and between them there is a general-special relation (umūm min wajh), because they coincide in the division of four and the whiteness of the surface, and the first applies without the second in the whiteness of a body, and conversely in the equality of the angles of a triangle to two right angles.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and explanation of the three properties of a part (juz'). The author states that a part has three special characteristics. The first property is priority in both mental and external existence; it is a real (haqiqiyya) property and does not apply to accidents. The second property is independence from an intermediary (wāsita) in assent: that is, the predicate is affirmed or denied of the subject without needing a mediating proof, simply by conceiving the quiddity. This is a relational (idafiyya) property because it can apply to evident concomitants. The third property is independence from a middle (wasṭ) in existence: the attribute is necessarily attached to the thing by itself without a middle term, whether the judgment of that attachment requires a middle (like the triangle's angle sum) or not (like the divisibility of four). The author then distinguishes between the second and third properties: independence from the middle makes the proposition primary, while independence from the intermediary makes the predicate primary. They have a general-special relation: they overlap in some cases but differ in others.

Key Terms
جزء
خاصة
تقدم
استغناء عن الواسطة
استغناء عن الوسط
قضية أولية
محمول أولي
عوارض
لوازم بيئة
أعراض أولوية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The second and third properties are the same.
Correction: The second property concerns independence from an intermediary in assent (making the predicate primary), while the third concerns independence from a middle in existence (making the proposition primary). They have a general-special relation but are distinct.

Checkpoint
Which of the three properties of a part is considered a real (ḥaqīqiyya) property?
A) Independence from the intermediary in assent
B) Priority in mind and external existence
C) Independence from the middle in existence
D) All three are real

Key Takeaway
The three properties of a part are priority, independence from an intermediary in assent, and independence from a middle in existence; the first is real and the latter two are relational, with a specific relation between them.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider something you are absolutely certain of. Is that certainty based on a long chain of proofs, or on direct conception of the matter itself? How does this lesson challenge you to examine the foundation of your certainties?
?
The author distinguishes between real and relational properties. Think of a belief or conviction you hold that might actually be relational—dependent on circumstances—but you treat as real and unchanging. Can you identify such a belief and trace it back to its proper basis?

Objection and Answer on the First Property of a Part
Al-Taftazani refines the first property using bi-sharṭ and lā bi-sharṭ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani refutes an objection to the first property of a part by refining its foundation using the concepts of 'taken with a condition' and 'taken without a condition'.

Core Idea
Priority is not just about existence; it depends on how a part is considered—as matter or as genus.

In this passage, al-Taftazani addresses an objection claiming that the first property of a part is merely relational, not real. He first acknowledges the objection's basis but then offers a refined answer based on the notions of 'bi-sharṭ' and 'lā bi-sharṭ', showing that the property is indeed real though not universal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argumentative flow come through.
Notice the objection-and-answer (qīl wa qul) structure.
2
Identify the objection
The objector gives two alternatives for what the first property might mean. Find both alternatives and understand why each supposedly leads to the property being relational.
The objector wants to show the property cannot be real.
3
Trace the author's response
The author first mentions the standard response based on the natural universal. Then he offers a refined foundation. Notice the shift from the universal to the bi-sharṭ / lā bi-sharṭ distinction.
The refined answer avoids the problems of the natural universal.
4
Analyze the two ways
The author presents two ways a part can have priority: as matter (taken bi-sharṭ) in external existence, or as genus/differentia (taken lā bi-sharṭ) in mental existence. Understand how each makes the property real.
5
Reflect on the scope
The author concludes that the property is real but not universal, because some parts (like hyle and form) lack priority in one realm. Consider why this is important.
This refines the definition without abandoning it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: إن أريد بالخاصة الأولى التقدم في الوجودين جميعاً على ما ظاهر عبارة القوم، فباطل لأن الجزء الذهني كالجنس والفصل لا يتقدم في الوجود العيني، وإلا امتنع الحمل، وإن أريد أن الجزء الذهني متقدم بالوجود الذهني والعيني على ما ذكر فالعلة الفاعلية للشيء متقدمة عليه في الخارج، إن كانت علة له في الخارج وفي الذهن إن كانت في الذهن فهذه الخاصة أيضاً، تكون إضافية لا حقيقية. قلنا : الظاهر أن مرادهم الأول على ما صرح به الإمام، ومبناه على ما تقرر عندهم من وجود الكلي الطبيعي، لكونه جزءاً من الأشخاص، وإذ قد بينا بطلان ذلك . = فالأولى : ابتناؤها على ما ذكرنا من أن الجزء أي ما يعرض له الجزئية متقدم بالوجودين إما بالوجود العيني، فباعتبار كونه مادة لكونه مأخوذاً بشرط . وإما بالوجود الذهني، فباعتبار كونه جنساً أو فصلاً لكونه مأخوذاً لا بشرط فتكون الخاصة حقيقية غير صادقة على العلة الفاعلية غاية الأمر أنها لا تكون شاملة بناء على أن من الأجزاء ما لا تقدم له في الخارج كلونية السواد أو في الذهن كالهيولي والصورة أو الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ إذا جوزنا تعقل حقيقة الجسم دون ذلك
For if it is said: If by the first property is meant priority in both existences, as the apparent wording of the people (al-qawm), then it is false, because the mental part, like genus (jins) and differentia (faṣl), does not precede in external existence, otherwise predication (ḥaml) would be impossible. And if it is meant that the mental part precedes by mental and external existence as mentioned, then the efficient cause ('illa fā'iliyya) of a thing precedes it in the external if it is a cause of it in the external, and in the mind if it is in the mind; then this property would also be relational, not real. We say: The apparent meaning is the first, as the Imam (al-Imām) explicitly stated, and it is based on what is established with them regarding the existence of the natural universal (kullī ṭabī'ī), being a part of individuals. And since we have shown the falsity of that, it is better to base it on what we mentioned: that the part (juz'), i.e., what accrues to it the state of being a part (juz'iyya), precedes by both existences. Either by external existence, in consideration of its being matter (māda) because it is taken with a condition (bi-sharṭ), or by mental existence, in consideration of its being a genus or differentia because it is taken without a condition (lā bi-sharṭ). Then the property becomes real, not applying to the efficient cause, except that it is not universal (shāmila), because among parts there are those that have no priority in external, like the colorness of blackness, or in the mind, like hyle (hayūlā) and form (ṣūra), or the indivisible parts if we allow conceiving the reality of a body without them.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (qīl-wa-qul) centered on the first property of a part. The objector argues: If the first property means priority in both existences, then mental parts like genus and differentia do not precede in external existence, making predication impossible. Alternatively, if mental parts do precede in both, then efficient causes also precede, making the property relational, not real. The author responds by stating that the intended meaning is the first interpretation, based on the existence of the natural universal. However, since that notion has been refuted, a better foundation is offered: the part, considered as what acquires the state of being a part, is prior in both existences—either as matter taken with a condition (bi-sharṭ) in external existence, or as genus/differentia taken without a condition (lā bi-sharṭ) in mental existence. On this view, the property is real and does not apply to efficient causes, though it is not universal because some parts (like the colorness of blackness, hyle, form, or indivisible atoms) lack priority in one of the realms.

حقيقية
إضافية
جزء ذهني
جزء عيني
كلي طبيعي
مادة

فصل
علة فاعلية
بشرط
لا بشرط
هيولي
صورة
جزء لا يتجزأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first property is either purely real and applies to all parts, or it is merely relational and has no real basis.
Correction: The author shows it is real when the part is considered in a specific way (as matter or genus), but not universal because some parts lack priority in one realm.

Checkpoint
According to the author's refined answer, when does the first property become real?
A) When the part is taken as matter with a condition
B) When the part is taken as genus or differentia without a condition
C) Both A and B
D) Only when the part is a natural universal

Key Takeaway
The first property of a part is real when the part is considered either as matter (taken with a condition) or as genus/differentia (taken without a condition), though it does not apply universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even in technical metaphysics, truth can be conditional—what is real under one consideration may not be under another. Where in your own understanding of Islamic doctrines (e.g., divine attributes, human responsibility) have you assumed something is absolute when it is actually conditional? Will you commit to re-examining that assumption today?
?
Al-Taftazani refuses to accept a false dichotomy: the first property is not merely relational, but it is not absolutely universal either. In your life, are there areas where you have forced yourself into a false choice (e.g., between strict literalism and complete relativism)? How will you adopt the nuanced 'real but conditional' approach that this lesson models?

Real Composition and the Dependence of Parts
Al-Taftazani defines real composition and its necessary implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of real composition in kalam and why it necessarily implies the dependence of parts on one another.

Core Idea
In real composition, parts need each other to exist.

In this brief passage, Al-Taftazani introduces a key distinction in kalam ontology: real composition (tarkīb ḥaqīqī) versus mere aggregation. He asserts that when something is truly composite, its parts are not independent but require one another. This principle underpins many theological arguments about the contingency of the world. Let's examine his statement and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the concise style and the two examples given.
The Arabic ends abruptly with 'أمر'—we have the intended meaning from context.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that real composition 'necessitates the need of some parts for others'. This is the core idea. Understand what 'real composition' means here.
Real composition is not just putting things together; it's when parts are ontologically interdependent.
3
Analyze the examples
The first example is the form of a composite (like a statue) which depends on its material parts. The second is the genus (like 'animal') which is a part of the definition and depends on the species difference. See how each pair shows mutual need.
These examples illustrate the principle in different domains: physical form/matter and logical genus/differentia.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والتركيب قد يكون حقيقياً فيلزم احتياج بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض، كصورة المركب المتقومة بأجزائه المادية، وكالجنس الذي هو أمر
He said: And composition (tarkīb) may be real (ḥaqīqī), and then it necessitates the need of some parts for others, like the form of a composite (ṣūrat al-murakkab) constituted by its material parts, and like the genus which is a thing.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a brief assertion about real composition (tarkīb ḥaqīqī). The author, quoting another source (possibly Imam al-Razi or someone else), states that composition can be real, and when it is, it necessitates that some parts need others. Two examples are given: first, the form of a composite (ṣūrat al-murakkab) which is constituted by its material parts—here the form depends on the matter; second, the genus (jins) which is something—likely meaning that the genus as a part of the definition depends on the differentia or species. The passage underscores that in genuine composition, parts are not independent but require each other, in contrast to mere aggregation.

Key Terms

احتياج
جزء
صورة مركب
جنس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Composition means parts are independent and can exist separately.
Correction: Real composition entails that parts need each other; they are not independent.

Checkpoint
What does real composition necessitate?
A) The independence of all parts
B) The need of some parts for others
C) The priority of all parts
D) The equality of all parts

Key Takeaway
Real composition implies that parts are interdependent; without each other, they cannot fulfill their role in the composite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that real composition means parts need each other. Consider your own life: are there areas where you pride yourself on being self-sufficient? How does the reality of your dependence on Allah and others—like form depends on matter—change how you should view your daily actions and relationships? What is one step you can take today to acknowledge this dependence?
?
The text gives the example of a genus needing its differentia. In your journey of faith, how is your understanding of tawheed (like the genus) incomplete without the specifics of worship (like the differentia)? Will you commit to studying the details that make your faith a true composite, not just a vague concept?
?
If real composition implies need, then anything truly composed is not independent. Your very existence is a composite of body and soul, time and actions. Are you living as if you are a self-contained unit, or do you recognize that every part of your life requires the other parts and ultimately depends on Allah? What would change if you truly internalized this lesson today?

6 min
The Proper Meaning of the Part and Its Priority
The author refutes a false view about the priority of parts and offers a corrected framework.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct meaning of 'part' (juz') in relation to its priority in external or mental existence, and why a common false view fails.

Core Idea
The priority of a part depends on how it is considered, not on a single mode of existence.

This passage tackles a common confusion in kalam and logic: how can a 'part' be said to precede the whole? Some thinkers assumed that mental parts like genus and differentia must precede in external existence, which leads to absurdities. The author refutes this and establishes a nuanced position based on the distinction between taking a part 'with a condition' or 'without a condition'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the author begins by dismissing a false view and then offers a corrective.
2
Identify the false view and its problem
The author states that the apparent meaning of 'the people' is false. Why? Because the mental part does not precede in external existence; if it did, predication would be impossible. Alternatively, if one claims it precedes both externally and mentally, then the efficient cause would also precede, making the property relative.
The false view is rooted in assuming the existence of the natural universal as a part of individuals.
3
Understand the correction
The author proposes a better foundation: the part is taken either with a condition (bi-shart) as matter, giving it priority in external existence, or without a condition (la bi-shart) as genus or differentia, giving it priority in mental existence. This makes the property 'priority' real but not universal.
Some parts (like blackness of black, matter/form) have no such priority at all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ظاهر عبارة القوم، فباطل لأن الجزء الذهني كالجنس والفصل لا يتقدم في الوجود العيني، وإلا امتنع الحمل، وإن أريد أن الجزء الذهني متقدم بالوجود الذهني والعيني على ما ذكر فالعلة الفاعلية للشيء متقدمة عليه في الخارج، إن كانت علة له في الخارج وفي الذهن إن كانت في الذهن فهذه الخاصة أيضاً، تكون إضافية لا حقيقية. قلنا: الظاهر أن مرادهم الأول على ما صرح به الإمام، ومبناه على ما تقرر عندهم من وجود الكلي الطبيعي، لكونه جزءاً من الأشخاص، وإذ قد بينا بطلان ذلك. فالأولى: ابتناؤها على ما ذكرنا من أن الجزء أي ما يعرض له الجزئية متقدم بالوجودين إما بالوجود العيني، فباعتبار كونه مادة لكونه مأخوذاً بشرط، وإما بالوجود الذهني، فباعتبار كونه جنساً أو فصلاً لكونه مأخوذاً لا بشرط فتكون الخاصة حقيقية غير صادقة على العلة الفاعلية غاية الأمر أنها لا تكون شاملة بناء على أن من الأجزاء ما لا تقدم له في الخارج كلونية السواد أو في الذهن كالهيولي والصورة أو الأجزاء التي لا تتجزأ إذا جوزنا تعقل حقيقة الجسم دون ذلك.
The apparent meaning of the people is false, because the mental part like the genus and the differentia does not precede in external existence; otherwise predication would be impossible. And if it is intended that the mental part precedes in mental and external existence as mentioned, then the efficient cause of a thing precedes it in the outside, if it is a cause for it in the outside, and in the mind if it is in the mind; so this property also becomes relative, not real. We say: The apparent is that their intended meaning is the first, as al-Imam stated, and its basis is what is established with them of the existence of the natural universal, because it is a part of the individuals, and since we have shown the falsity of that. So the better [foundation] is to base it on what we mentioned: that the part – that is, what is subject to part-hood – precedes in both existences: either in external existence, by virtue of its being matter, because it is taken with a condition, or in mental existence, by virtue of its being a genus or a differentia, because it is taken without a condition. So the property is real, not applying to the efficient cause; the only point is that it is not universal, based on the fact that among parts are those that have no priority in the outside, like the blackness of black? or in the mind like matter and form, or the indivisible parts if we allow conceiving the reality of the body without that.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a refutation followed by a correction. The author begins by presenting a false view: that the mental part (like genus and differentia) does not precede in external existence, and if it did, predication would be impossible. He notes that if one instead claims that the mental part precedes in both mental and external existence, then the efficient cause would precede in existence, making the property relative. The author then clarifies that the intended meaning (of 'the people') is the first interpretation, which is based on the existence of the natural universal as a part of individuals—but this has already been shown false. Therefore, the author proposes a better foundation: the part is taken either with a condition (bi-shart) as matter, giving it priority in external existence, or without a condition (la bi-shart) as genus or differentia, giving it priority in mental existence. Thus the property 'priority' is real but not universal, because some parts (like the blackness of black in external existence or matter/form in mental existence) have no such priority.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that the priority of a part always means priority in external existence.
Correction: The priority can be in mental existence when the part is taken without a condition (as genus or differentia).

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines whether a part has priority in external or mental existence?
A) Whether it is a genus or a differentia
B) Whether it is taken with a condition (bi-shart) or without a condition (la bi-shart)
C) Whether it is a material part or a formal part
D) Whether it is a part of a natural universal

Key Takeaway
The correct view: a part's priority is not fixed but depends on whether it is considered with a condition (material) or without condition (logical).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, do you tend to think that one thing (like intention or action) always comes first, or that there is only one way to prioritize? How does this lesson's distinction between considering a part 'with condition' or 'without condition' challenge you to hold multiple perspectives at once? What would it look like for you to apply this to your relationship with Allah—for example, in balancing hope and fear, or knowledge and practice?
?
Reflect on a time when you argued with someone about the priority of something (e.g., which is more important, belief or deeds). Could your disagreement have been rooted in not distinguishing how the thing is being considered (material vs. logical)? Think honestly: are you willing to re-examine that conflict in light of this lesson, and if so, what would you say differently now?

Real Composition and the Need of Parts
The author defines real composition and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of real composition and why it necessitates the interdependence of parts, as illustrated by material composites and the genus-differentia relationship.

Core Idea
Real composition means parts depend on each other; the genus is indeterminate without its differentia.

In this passage, the author introduces the notion of real composition, where the parts of a composite necessarily require each other. He gives two key examples: a material form that depends on its parts, and the genus that only becomes a species through the differentia. This sets the stage for understanding how logical terms like genus and differentia relate in reality and in the mind.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on real composition
Read the passage once to get the overall meaning. Notice the definition and the two examples.
2
Identify the two examples of real composition
The author gives two examples: a material composite (like a physical object) and the genus-differentia relationship. Identify each.
3
Understand the consequence: no external distinction
The author states that without real composition, there would be no distinction in the outside world between genus and differentia, nor between species and individual. The distinction is only in the mind.
For instance, Zayd is both human and rational animal.
4
Note the one-sided need in some composites
The author explains that the need between parts can be one-sided, as in the case of form needing matter but not vice versa.
5
Reflect on the counterexample of the stone
The author says a stone placed beside a human does not form a single essence, emphasizing that real composition requires genuine interdependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والتركيب قد يكون حقيقياً فيلزم احتياج بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض، كصورة المركب المتقومة بأجزائه المادية، وكالجنس الذي هو أمر مبهم لا يتحصل نوعاً حقيقياً، إلا بمقارنة الفصل. وهذا معنى عليته، وإلا فلا تمايز في الخارج بين الجنس والفصل بل النوع والشخص أيضاً، فزيد هو الإنسان والحيوان الناطق، وإنما التمايز في العقل من جهة أنه يحصل من الشيء صور متعددة باعتبارات مختلفة. بأن يحصل من اجتماع عدة أشياء حقيقة واحدة بالذات، مختصة باللوازم والإشارات واحتياج بعض أجزائه إلى البعض ضروري للقطع بأنه لا يحصل من الحجر الموضوع بجنب الإنسان حقيقة واحدة، والاحتياج فيما بين الجزأين، قد يكون من جانب واحد كالمركب من البسائط العنصرية، ومما يقوم بها عن الصورة المعدنية أو النباتية، أو الحيوانية. فإن الصورة تحتاج إلى تلك المواد من غير عكس، وكالمركب من الجنس والفصل، فإن الجنس محتاج إلى الفصل من جهة أنه أمر مبهم لا يتحصل معقولاً مطابقاً لما في الأعيان من الأنواع الحقيقية إلا إذا اقترن به فصل، لأنه الذي يحصل طبيعة الجنس ويقررها، ويعينها ويقومها نوعاً. وهذا معنى علية الفصل للجنس، وحاصله أنه الذي به يتحصص الجنس أي يصير حصة حصة ولذا نقل الإمام عن أبي علي أن الفصل علة لحصة النوع من الجنس، وإن كان صريح عبارته أنه علة لطبيعة الجنس، بمعنى أن الصورة الجنسية ليست متحصلة بنفسها، بل مبهمة محتملة لأن تقال على أشياء مختلفة الحقائق، وإذا انضافت إليها الصورة الفعلية تحصلت وصارت بعينها أحد تلك الأشياء. فالفصل بالحقيقة علة لتحصلها بهذا المعنى، وارتفاع إبهامها لحصولها في العقل، لظهور أن المعنى الجنسي يعقل من غير فصل.
He said: And composition may be real, so it necessitates the need of some parts for others, like the form of the composite constituted by its material parts, and like the genus which is an indeterminate thing that does not become a real species except by the conjunction of the differentia. And this is the meaning of its causality; otherwise there would be no distinction in the outside between genus and differentia, nor even between species and individual. So Zayd is the human and the rational animal; distinction is only in the mind, because from a thing multiple forms are obtained according to different considerations. Because from the conjunction of several things a single essence in itself comes to be, specified by necessary concomitants and indications, and the need of some of its parts for others is necessary, because it is certain that from a stone placed beside a human no single essence results. And the need between the two parts may be one-sided, like the composite of elementary simples and what subsists by them from the mineral, plant, or animal form. For the form needs those materials without the reverse. And like the composite of genus and differentia: the genus needs the differentia because it is an indeterminate thing that does not become an intelligible matching the real species in the external world unless a differentia is conjoined to it, because it is the one that obtains the nature of the genus, determines it, specifies it, and constitutes it as a species. And this is the meaning of the causality of the differentia for the genus, and its gist is that it is that by which the genus becomes particularized, i.e., becomes a particular share. Hence al-Imam transmitted from Abu Ali that the differentia is the cause of the share of the species from the genus, although the explicit wording of his statement is that it is the cause of the nature of the genus, meaning that the generic form is not obtained by itself, but is indeterminate, susceptible of being said of things of different realities. And when the actual form (the differentia) is added to it, it becomes obtained and becomes exactly one of those things. So the differentia in reality is the cause of its being obtained in this sense, and the removal of its indeterminacy, because it is obtained in the mind, since it is obvious that the generic meaning is conceived without the differentia.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of real composition (tarkib haqiqi) and the causal relationship between genus and differentia. The author begins by quoting a statement: composition may be real, necessitating the need of some parts for others. Two examples are given: (1) the form of a composite that depends on its material parts, and (2) the genus, which is an indeterminate entity that only becomes a real type (species) when joined with a differentia. This expresses the causality of the differentia. Without such causality, there would be no distinction in the external world between genus and differentia, nor even between species and individual; e.g., Zayd is both the human and the rational animal—the distinctions are mental only. The author then explains that real composition occurs when several things combine into a single essence with its own properties, and the interdependence of parts is essential (unlike a stone beside a human). The need can be one-sided, as in the case of elemental simples where the form needs matter but not vice versa, or in the case of genus and differentia, where the genus needs the differentia to become determinate. The differentia is thus the cause of the genus's actuality as a species: it particularizes the genus into a specific share. The author notes that al-Imam (al-Razi) quoted Abu Ali (Ibn Sina) saying that the differentia is the cause of the species' share from the genus, though Abu Ali's own wording said it is the cause of the nature of the genus. The author clarifies that this means the generic form is not inherently determinate; it becomes determinate when the differentia joins it, making it one of those things. Thus the differentia is truly the cause of the genus's determinacy in the mind, since the generic meaning can be conceived without the differentia (as an indeterminate concept).

Key Terms
التركيب الحقيقي
الجزء
الجنس
الفصل
العلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Genus and differentia are two distinct things in external reality.
Correction: In reality, there is no separate existence for genus and differentia; they are mental distinctions. For example, Zayd is both human and rational animal—they are the same entity.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is there no distinction between genus and differentia in the external world?
A) Because they are identical in meaning
B) Because distinction is only in the mind
C) Because genus does not exist
D) Because differentia is part of genus

Key Takeaway
Real composition involves essential interdependence of parts; genus and differentia are distinguished only mentally, not in reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that real composition involves parts that need each other. Look at your own identity as a believer: are your knowledge and practice truly interdependent, or do you treat them as separate? In what one area will you deliberately unite them starting today?
?
The author says that without real interdependence, no single essence results—like a stone beside a human. Is your relationship with Allah a real composition where every part (heart, tongue, limbs) depends on the others, or is it a mere juxtaposition? Where do you feel the disconnect?
?
If genus and differentia are only mentally distinct but one in reality, what does that say about your own divisions between 'religion' and 'world'? Will you stop treating them as separate boxes and instead see them as one integrated life? What is the first step?

The Differentia as Cause of the Genus
The author explains the causality of the differentia for the genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific causal relationship between genus and differentia, and how the differentia determines the genus into a species.

Core Idea
The differentia is the cause that makes the indeterminate genus into a determinate species.

In this passage, the author elaborates on the relationship between genus and differentia, focusing on the differentia's role in actualizing the genus. He explains that the genus is inherently indeterminate and requires the differentia to become a real species, and this is the meaning of the differentia's causality. He also clarifies a transmission from Imam al-Razi about Abu Ali's view.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage on genus and differentia
Read the passage carefully, noting the description of genus as indeterminate and the role of differentia.
2
Understand why genus needs differentia
The genus is indeterminate and does not become a real species unless a differentia is conjoined to it. The differentia obtains, determines, specifies, and constitutes the genus as a species.
3
Grasp the meaning of causality
The differentia is the cause because it particularizes the genus into a specific 'share' (hissa). This is the essence of its causality.
4
Examine the clarification from al-Imam and Abu Ali
Al-Imam transmitted from Abu Ali that the differentia is the cause of the share of the species from the genus, although Abu Ali's wording was that it is the cause of the nature of the genus. The author reconciles this by noting that the generic form is indeterminate and becomes determinate only with the differentia.
5
Reflect on the generic meaning without differentia
The author concludes that the generic meaning can be conceived without the differentia, but the differentia removes its indeterminacy. This shows that the causality of the differentia is about determination, not creation.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The differentia is a separate entity that acts on the genus externally.
Correction: The differentia is not a separate entity; it is a conceptual factor that makes the genus determinate. The genus and differentia are in reality the same thing, distinguished mentally.

What does the author mean by saying the differentia is the cause of the genus?

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often say 'I believe in Allah'—but is that belief merely a generic concept, an indeterminate genus without the differentia of detailed knowledge and action? What specific truth about Allah will you internalize today to make your faith more than a mental abstraction?
?
The differentia brings the genus from vagueness to concrete reality. Where in your life—your character, your worship, your relationships—do you tolerate indeterminacy? What specific 'differentia' will you apply tomorrow to turn an intention into an action?
?
You can conceive of justice without specifying its details, but without the differentia of Islamic rulings, it remains hollow. Are you satisfied with a generic notion of right and wrong, or will you commit to learning the precise determinations of Shari'ah that give your moral sense real content?

Genus, Differentia, and Species: Mental Distinctions Only
The author argues for the mental distinction of genus, differentia, and species.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why genus, differentia, and species are considered distinct only in the mind, not in external reality, and how the author addresses an objection from composite species.

Core Idea
Genus, differentia, and species are mental constructs, not separate external components.

This passage tackles a foundational issue in logic: are the parts of a definition (genus, differentia) actually separate in reality? The author argues they are not—only individuals exist externally. Through careful reasoning and an example of a horse's death, he defends the purely conceptual nature of these logical distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then follow along with the translation. Notice the flow from statement to objection to answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that there is no distinction between genus and differentia in external reality. Find where he says this and underline it.
Look for the phrase 'لا تمايز في الخارج'.
3
Examine the causality of the differentia
The author explains how the differentia 'causes' the genus to become determinate. Understand that this causality is logical, not physical.
This is key to the mental distinction.
4
Analyze the objection and answer
The objection claims that in composite species like animal, body and soul are distinct externally. See how the author distinguishes between matter and genus.
The answer clarifies what is meant by 'essential attributes'.
5
Formulate the takeaway
Conclude that logical categories (genus, differentia, species) are mental tools, not external parts. Write a one-sentence summary.

وجعل الجسم غير جعل النفس، حتى إذا زالت عنه النفس بقي ذلك الجسم بعينه موجوداً كالفرس الذي يموت وجسميته باقية ولهذا يصح أن يقال: جعل جسماً فجعل حيواناً . قلنا : الجسم المأخوذ على وجه كونه مادة غير المأخوذ على وجه كونه جنساً بالذاتي (۲). [ ولا كلام في تميز الأول عن الكل بالوجود الخارجي، وإنما الكلام في الثاني، لأنه الجزء المحمول المسمى بالذاتي ](٣) . وقد سبق تحقيق ذلك. والحاصل أن الذاتيات المتمايزة بحسب العقل فقط، قد يكون لها مبادىء
It is vague and does not become a real species except by association with the differentia. This is the meaning of its being caused; otherwise there would be no distinction in external reality between genus and differentia, nor between species and individual either. For Zayd is the human and the rational animal; rather the distinction is in the mind, in that multiple images are obtained from a thing according to different considerations. Such that from the conjunction of several things a single essence in itself is obtained, specified by properties and indications, and the need of some of its parts for others is necessary, for it is certain that from a stone placed beside a human a single essence is not obtained. The need between the two parts may be one-sided, like the composite from elemental simples and what subsists by them from the mineral, plant, or animal form. For the form needs those materials without the reverse; and like the composite from genus and differentia, for the genus needs the differentia in that it is a vague matter which does not become an intelligible matching the real species in concrete individuals unless a differentia is joined to it, because it is that which actualizes the nature of the genus, establishes it, specifies it, and constitutes it as a species. This is the meaning of the causality of the differentia for the genus. Its summary: that it is that by which the genus becomes particularized, i.e., becomes a specific portion. Hence the Imam reported from Abu Ali that the differentia is a cause for the portion of the species from the genus, even though the explicit statement of his expression is that it is a cause for the nature of the genus, meaning that the generic form is not actualized by itself, but is vague and possible to be said of things of different realities, and when the actual form is added to it, it becomes actualized and becomes exactly one of those things. So the differentia in reality is a cause for its actualization in this sense, and the removal of its vagueness is due to its existence in the mind, since it is apparent that the generic meaning is understood without a differentia, not due to its existence externally, because there is no distinction between them externally. Otherwise, the predication of one upon the other by synonymy would be impossible. And it is clear that there is not in blackness a determined thing that is color and another that is the repulsiveness of sight such that they combine and blackness results. Rather, the truth is that there is nothing externally except individuals, and genus, differentia, and species are distinct images in the mind, which it obtains from the individual according to predispositions that occur to the mind and considerations it conceives from particulars fewer or more, differing in dissimilarity and commonality. Thus it perceives from Zayd sometimes a personal image such that no one else shares it with him, and another an image in which ‘Amr and Bakr share with him, and another an image in which the horse and others share with him, and so on. If it is said: This is only in the simple species like blackness, for it is apparent that there is not in external reality a color-ness and something else by which blackness is distinguished from other colors, and for this it is not correct to say: He made it a color, then made it black. Rather, He made them both one. But in other than it, the essential attributes distinct in the mind are distinct in external reality, and their making is not one, like animal: for it shares with plant in being a body and is distinguished from it by the animal soul, and the making of the body is different from the making of the soul, such that when the soul leaves it, that same body remains existing, like the horse that dies and its bodiliness remains, and for this it is correct to say: He made it a body, then made it an animal. We say: The body taken as being matter is different from it taken as being a genus in essence. And there is no discussion about the distinction of the first from everything by external existence; rather the discussion is about the second, because it is the predicated part called essential. And the investigation of that has preceded. The conclusion is that the essential attributes distinct only according to the mind may have principles...
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage is a complex argument about the ontological status of genus (جنس), differentia (فصل), and species (نوع). It begins by stating that the differentia gives actuality to the genus, but then argues that in external reality there is no distinction between genus and differentia, nor between species and individual (شخص). Zayd is both the human and the rational animal; the distinction is only mental (عقلي), arising from multiple considerations of the same thing. The author illustrates with the composition of a whole from parts: a stone beside a human does not form a single essence, showing that true composition requires mutual need. In composite things like a horse, the form needs matter, but not vice versa. Similarly, genus needs differentia to become determinate. The differentia is thus a 'cause' for the genus's specification. The argument then shifts: the generic meaning is understood without differentia in the mind, but externally they are indistinguishable. If they were distinct externally, you could not predicate one of the other synonymously (بالمواطأة). Using the example of blackness (سواد), which is both a color and a repulsive property, the author insists that these are not separate external components but one thing. Only individuals exist externally; genus, differentia, and species are mental images (صور) derived from individuals. An objection is then raised: this works for simple species like blackness, but for composite species like animal, the essential attributes (ذاتيات)—like body and soul—seem externally distinct since the body remains after death. The author answers by distinguishing between 'matter' and 'genus': the body taken as matter is different from body taken as a genus (a logical part). The former is indeed externally distinct, but the latter (the logical genus) is not. The conclusion is that the essential attributes distinct only in the mind may have real principles, but the logical categories are mental constructs.

فصل
نوع
شخص
ذاتيات
عقل
خارج

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that genus and differentia are separate components in external reality, like body and soul in an animal.
Correction: The author clarifies that genus and differentia are only conceptually distinct; in reality, only individuals exist. Even in composite species, the logical parts are mental constructs, not external parts.

Checkpoint
What is the author's main contention about the distinction between genus and differentia in external reality?
A) They are distinct in external reality like body and soul in animals.
B) They are distinct only in the mind, not externally.
C) They are distinct in both mind and external reality for composite species.
D) They are not distinct even in the mind.

Key Takeaway
Genus, differentia, and species are mental constructs used to understand individuals, not separate components of external reality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call yourself 'Muslim,' 'human,' or any label, do you realize that these are mental constructs that capture only part of your reality? In front of Allah, you are not a collection of categories but a unique individual. How does this realization change the way you approach your relationship with Him—not as a checklist of attributes but as a direct, personal connection? Will you today set aside the labels and speak to Him as your singular self?
?
The author says there is nothing externally except individuals. You often judge people by their group identity (race, nationality, sect). But in reality, each person is a unique creation. Can you look at someone you dislike and see them as an individual, not as a category? What would it take for you to see past the mental abstraction of 'the other' and encounter the actual person? Commit to one concrete action this week that treats someone as an individual, not a label.
?
If the differentia only causes the genus mentally, then your 'essence' is not a fixed concept but a living reality that only Allah fully knows. Are you trying to define yourself by a mental formula (e.g., 'I am a sinner,' 'I am righteous') rather than living in the presence of Allah who sees you as you are? What would it mean to stop reducing yourself to a concept and instead present your actual, messy existence to Him in prayer? This lesson invites you to abandon self-definitions and simply be before Allah.

The Simple Type and Privative Differentia
Author defines the simple type and discusses possibility of privative differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the simple type (al-naw' al-basit) and how it allows for the possibility of privative differentia in logical definitions.

Core Idea
A simple type composite has parts that are not distinct in external existence.

This passage introduces a key distinction in logic: the 'simple type' of composite, where components are not externally separate but only conceptually distinguished. The author then uses this to show that the differentia that defines a species can be a privation rather than a positive attribute. Understanding this nuance is essential for grasping how definitions work in Islamic logic and ontology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the flow from examples to the argument about privative differentia.
The passage is compact; focus on the examples first.
2
Identify the examples of the simple type
The passage gives three examples: blackness from color, the power of grasping from vision, and surface from continuous quantity. Reflect on why these are considered 'simple' composites.
In each case, the parts are conceptually distinct but not separate in reality.
3
Understand the argument for privative differentia
The author notes that some investigators allowed the differentia to be privative. The proof: continuous quantity gives surface (length+width) and line (length only). The differentia that makes line a line is the privation of width.
This argument relies on the simple type structure of quantity.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this matters: definitions do not always require positive attributes; privations can serve as differentiae. This broadens the scope of what can be defined.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد لا يكون كالسواد من اللون، وقابضية البصر؛ وكالسطح من الكم، وقابليته القسمة في الطول والعرض جميعاً، وهو المسمى بالنوع البسيط، ومن ههنا جوز بعض المحققين كون الفصل عدمياً فإن المعنى الجنسي من الكم المتصل، يتحصل بماله من طول وعرض فقط، فيكون سطحاً، وبما له طول فقط فيكون خطاً.
And it may not be [distinct externally] like blackness from color, and [like] the power of grasping from vision; and like surface from quantity and its divisibility in length and breadth together, and it is called the simple type. And from here some investigators allowed that the differentia could be privative, for the generic meaning of continuous quantity is constituted by what has length and breadth only, so it becomes a surface, and by what has length only so it becomes a line.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the 'simple type' (al-naw' al-basit) of composite where the parts are not distinct externally. Examples: blackness is a color but not a distinct part of color; the power of grasping is part of vision but not separately existing; surface is a quantity divisible in two dimensions, but it is a simple type because surface and line are not separate entities in external reality — they are conceptually derived from continuous quantity. The author then notes that some investigators (muhaqqiqun) allowed that the differentia (fasl) in such composites can be privative ('adami). The proof: the generic concept of continuous quantity (al-kamm al-muttasil) is actualized by having length and width only (giving surface) or by having length only (giving line). Since line is the privation of width, the differentia that distinguishes line from surface is privative. Thus the differentia need not be a positive attribute.

Key Terms
النوع البسيط
الفصل العدمي
الكم المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'simple type' means that the composite is simple (indivisible).
Correction: The simple type refers to a composite whose parts are not distinct in external existence; the composite itself is not simple but its parts are only conceptually separable.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'simple type' composite according to the passage?
A) A heap of stones
B) Surface (al-sath) from continuous quantity
C) A tree with roots and branches
D) A mixture of water and oil

Key Takeaway
The simple type shows that some composites have only conceptual parts, and this allows differentiae to be privative rather than positive attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever defined yourself by what you lack rather than what you possess? For example, do you think of yourself as 'not angry' or 'not arrogant' rather than as 'patient' or 'humble'? How does this lesson's concept of privative differentia challenge or affirm that way of thinking about your own character?
?
The simple type shows that conceptual distinctions do not always correspond to real separations. In your own life, are there beliefs or attachments you treat as separate 'parts' when they are really just aspects of a deeper core? How might recognizing this change the way you evaluate yourself before Allah?
?
Some investigators accepted privative differentiae. Are there areas in your faith where you rely on what is absent (e.g., absence of doubt, absence of sin) as a defining marker? What would it mean for your worship if you instead focused on positive attributes like certainty and obedience?

Conceptual Composites and No Dependence
Sanusi defines the conceptual composite and clarifies its logical implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a conceptual composite and why its parts do not depend on each other in reality.

Core Idea
Conceptual composites are mental unities; their parts exist independently.

Sanusi now turns to the third type of composite: the conceptual composite. Unlike real composites where parts may depend on each other, conceptual composites are unities only in the mind. Using the example of an army, he explains how many individual things are considered one by the intellect without any real interdependence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to get a clear sense of the author's wording.
2
Identify the key definition
Locate where Sanusi defines conceptual composite: 'several matters that the intellect considers as one thing, even if not one in reality.'
3
Examine the examples
Notice the examples: army and ten. Ask yourself how these are 'made one' by the intellect despite having independent parts.
The army example is central.
4
Understand the implication
Grasp why no dependence is necessary. Since the unity is mental, the parts can exist without each other.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وقد يكون اعتبارياً كالعسكر فلا يلزم. بأن يكون هناك عدة أمور يعتبرها العقل أمراً واحداً، وإن لم يكن أمراً واحداً في الحقيقة. وربما يضع بإزائه اسماً، كالعشرة من الأحاد، والعسكر من الأفراد، ولا يلزم فيه احتياج بعض الأجزاء إلى البعض.
He said: And it may be conceptual, like an army, so no dependence is necessary. That is, there are several matters that the intellect considers as one thing, even if it is not one thing in reality. And sometimes it assigns a name to it, like ten from units, and an army from individuals, and no need of some parts for others is necessary in it.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage explains the third kind of composite: conceptual (i' tibari). The author gives the example of an army ('askar). An army is a composite of individual soldiers, but its unity is only mental — the intellect considers many individuals as one unit. Similarly, ten is a number composed of units. In such composites, there is no real dependence of the parts on each other; the parts exist independently. The phrase 'so no dependence is necessary' (fala yalzam) refers to the earlier discussion that in real composites parts may need each other, but here they do not.

Key Terms
المركب الاعتباري
العسكر
الاعتبار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think conceptual composites are purely imaginary and have no basis in reality.
Correction: The passage clarifies that conceptual composites are based on real individual parts (e.g., soldiers, units) but their unity is only mental, not external.

Checkpoint
Why does an army count as a conceptual composite?
A) Because its parts are not real.
B) Because its unity is only in the intellect, not externally.
C) Because soldiers depend on each other.
D) Because it is a simple entity.

Key Takeaway
Conceptual composites are mental unities composed of independent parts; the parts do not need each other.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

In what ways do you mistakenly treat that group as a single real entity, forgetting that each individual (including yourself) is independent and not inherently dependent on the others? How does this mistake cause you to neglect your own responsibility or the individuality of others?
?
The lesson says that in a conceptual composite, the parts do not need each other. Are there relationships or commitments in your life where you have assumed that others need you or you need them in a way that contradicts this truth? How can you adjust your expectations and behavior accordingly?

Dependence in Real vs Conceptual Composites
Sanusi refutes an objection about dependence by distinguishing real from conceptual composites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between real and conceptual composites in kalam and how it resolves objections about dependence.

Core Idea
Conceptual composites exist only in the mind; real composites have actual forms.

The objection argues that dependence is unavoidable in any composite. Al-Sanusi replies by distinguishing between composites that exist truly (real) and those that are merely mental (conceptual). This lesson unpacks that crucial distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objector presents two possibilities for 'no dependence'. Identify both and understand why each is problematic from the objector's perspective.
The objection hinges on the necessity of dependence in any composite.
2
Grasp the author's distinction
The author says the first meaning is intended and then distinguishes conceptual composites (no real form) from real composites (actual forms emanating onto matter). Note the example of an army vs. mineral compounds.
Conceptual composites like an army have no unifying external form.
3
Consider the unresolved case
The author hesitates about theriac and oxymel: are they real or conceptual? This shows that not all cases are settled.
This hesitation indicates ongoing debate in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل إن أريد عدم الاحتياج أصلاً فباطل، لأن احتياج الهيئة الاجتماعية إلى الأجزاء المادية لازم قطعاً، وإن أريد الاحتياج فيما بين الأجزاء المادية، فذلك ليس بلازم في المركب الحقيقي أيضاً، كالبسائط العنصرية للمركبات المعدنية مثلاً. قلنا المراد الأول، والصورة الاجتماعية في المركبات الاعتبارية محض اعتبار العقل، لا تحقق لها في الخارج، إذ ليس من العسكر في الخارج إلا تلك الأفراد، بخلاف المركبات الحقيقية، فإن هناك صوراً تفيض على المواد في نفس الأمر وستعرفها. وأما في مثل الترياق والسكنجين فهل يحدث صورة جوهرية هي مبدأ الآثار أو هو مجرد المزاج المخصوص الذي هو من قبيل الأعراض، وأن يكون التركيب الحقيقي هل يكون من الجوهر والعرض ففيه تردد.
For if it is said: If what is intended is no dependence at all, then it is false, because the dependence of the social whole on the material parts is absolutely necessary. And if what is intended is dependence among the material parts, then that is not necessary even in the real composite, like the elemental simple bodies for mineral compounds, for example. We say: The first is intended, and the social form in conceptual composites is a mere mental consideration, having no realization in external existence, since there is nothing of the army in external existence except those individuals, unlike real composites, for there are forms that emanate onto the matters in actual fact, and you will come to know them. As for things like theriac and oxymel, whether a substantial form arises that is the principle of effects, or it is merely the specific temperament which is of the category of accidents, and whether the real composition is from substance and accident, there is hesitation regarding this.
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This is an objection-and-answer passage. The objector argues: If by 'no dependence' you mean absolutely no dependence, that is false because a social whole (like a city) depends on its material parts (people). If you mean no dependence among the material parts themselves, that is also false because even in real composites like mineral compounds, the elemental simple bodies do not depend on each other. The author answers: The intended meaning is the first (absolute lack of dependence in conceptual composites). In conceptual composites, the 'social form' is purely mental — there is no real form uniting the parts externally; only the individuals exist. In real composites, there are real forms that flow onto matter. The author then adds a hesitation about compounds like theriac (tiriyaq) and oxymel (sikanjabin): whether they have a substantial form causing their effects or only a specific temperament (accidental), and whether real composition can be from substance and accident — this is undecided (mutaraddad).

Key Terms
الهيئة الاجتماعية
الصورة الاجتماعية
المركب الحقيقي
المركب الاعتباري
المزاج المخصوص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All composites have real unifying forms and are equally dependent on their parts.
Correction: Conceptual composites (e.g., an army) have no external form; only the parts exist externally. Real composites have actual forms that emanate onto matter.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes real composites from conceptual ones according to the author's answer?
A) Real composites have parts that are not dependent.
B) Real composites have a real form that emanates onto matter.
C) Conceptual composites have a social form.
D) Real composites are like armies.

Key Takeaway
In kalam, the distinction between real and conceptual composites resolves the problem of dependence: real composites have actual forms, while conceptual ones are merely mental constructs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You belong to many groups—your family, your community, your nation. The lesson says such collections have no real external unity; they are mental constructs. Does your attachment to these groups sometimes lead you to treat them as if they have an independent reality, perhaps even excusing your own failings by blaming 'the system'? How does this realization shift your personal responsibility before Allah?
?
The author distinguishes real composites (with emanating forms) from conceptual ones. What about your own soul and body—do you see yourself as a real composite with a substantial form (your soul) that unifies your body, or do you live as if you are merely a collection of roles and attributes? Which view demands more accountability in your actions?
?
The passage hesitates on theriac and oxymel—whether their effect comes from a substantial form or mere temperament. This shows that not all matters are settled. Is there an area of your religious practice where you have assumed certainty without knowing the basis? Will you commit today to investigating one such matter with humility, rather than relying on comfortable assumptions?

Classification of Composite Parts: Overlapping and Distinct
Author classifies parts of composite into overlapping and distinct with further subcategories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of parts of a composite into overlapping (mutadakhila) and distinct (mutabayina), and the subcategories under each.

Core Idea
Overlapping parts co-extend; distinct parts don't. Both have finer types.

In logic, when we analyze a composite (murakkab), its parts can relate to each other in different ways. Some parts overlap in meaning, covering the same or partly same referents; others are entirely distinct. The author here provides a systematic classification that helps in understanding how concepts compose. Let's explore the categories.

Guided Path
1
Understand the primary division
Read the initial division: parts either overlap (mutadakhila) or are distinct (mutabayina). Overlap means they have co-extensiveness (tasadiq).
This binary is the foundation.
2
Learn the three types of overlap
The passage lists three ways parts overlap: by equality (musāwa), by absolute generality (ʿumūm muṭlaq), and from one aspect (ʿumūm min wajh).
Equality means equal extension; absolute generality means one is broader; from one aspect means partial overlap.
3
Explore the subcategories of distinct parts
Distinct parts are further divided into pairs: similar/different, positive/privative, mixed/real, relative/blended.
These are multiple axes of classification, not a single hierarchy.
4
Review the complete classification schema
Try to reproduce the tree from memory, seeing how the categories relate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم أجزاء المركب إلى متداخلة ومتبانة. قال والأجزاء قد تتداخل بأن يكون بينها تصادق بالمساواة، أو العموم مطلقاً، أو من وجه، وقد تتباين متماثلة أو متخالفة، وجودية أو عدمية، أو مختلطة أو حقيقية، أو إضافية أو ممتزجة. أجزاء المركب تنقسم إلى متداخلة ومتباينة أما المتداخلة، فهي التي يكون...
Division of the parts of composite into overlapping and distinct. He said: And the parts may overlap, such that there is co-extensiveness between them by equality, or absolute generality, or from one aspect, and they may be distinct, similar or different, positive or privative, or mixed or real, or relative or blended. The parts of a composite are divided into overlapping and distinct. As for the overlapping, they are those that...
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of the parts (ajza') of a composite. First, they are divided into overlapping (mutadakhila) and distinct (mutabayina). Overlapping parts have co-extensiveness (tasadiq) in three ways: by equality (musawāt) — the parts apply to exactly the same extent; by absolute generality ('umūm mutlaq) — one part applies to everything the other applies to but not vice versa; or from one aspect ('umūm min wajh) — they overlap in some instances but not all. Distinct parts are those that do not overlap; they can be similar (mutamāthila) or different (mukhālifa); positive (wujūdiyya) or privative ('adamiyya); mixed (mukhtalita) — some positive some privative; real (haqīqiyya) — essential; relative (idāfiyya); or blended (mumtazija). The explanation of overlapping parts begins but is cut off.

Key Terms
المتداخلة
المتباينة
التصادق
المساواة
العموم المطلق
العموم من وجه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Overlapping parts are physically intertwined or spatially overlapping.
Correction: In logic, 'overlapping' (tadakhul) refers to conceptual co-extensiveness (tasāduq), meaning the parts apply to the same referents in some way, not physical overlap.

Checkpoint
How many types of overlapping (tadakhul) are listed?
A) Two: by equality and generality.
B) Three: by equality, absolute generality, and from one aspect.
C) Four: by equality, generality, aspect, and distinct.
D) Five: including positive and privative.

Key Takeaway
The parts of a composite are classified into overlapping (with three subtypes) and distinct (with multiple binary axes), providing a logical tool for analyzing concept composition.
